Types of Qadaa in Hanafi Usul
Classification of qadaa types with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of qadaa as classified in Hanafi usul, and apply the classification to new examples.

Core Idea
Qadaa may involve substitutes that are rationally similar, known only through Shariah, or that retain the meaning of performance.

This lesson examines the Hanafi classification of qadaa (making up missed obligations) into three categories based on the nature of the substitute. Understanding this classification is essential for recognizing how Shariah can prescribe substitutes that reason alone may not perceive, as exemplified by the Quranic allowance of fidyah for fasting. We will work through each type with clear examples and analyze the underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Types
Read the passage and list the three categories of qadaa mentioned: with a rational equivalent, with an irrational equivalent, and resembling performance (qadaa mushabih bil-adaa).
The author uses Arabic terms: qadaa bi-mithl al-ma'qool, qadaa bi-mithl ghayr al-ma'qool, qadaa mushabih bil-adaa.
2
Understand the Basis for Each Type
For each type, determine whether the similarity between the original obligation and its substitute is understood by reason alone or only through Shariah. Note that the third type retains the meaning of performance literally or constructively.
Fasting for a missed fast is rationally similar; fidyah for a missed fast is not; making up missed takbirs in ruku' retains the performance element.
3
Examine the Quranic Evidence
The passage cites Surah Al-Baqarah (2:184) as evidence for fidyah. Analyze how this verse supports the concept of qadaa bi-mithl ghayr al-ma'qool, and note that the verse has multiple interpretations as mentioned by the author of Nur al-Anwar.
The verse is Quranic (qat'i thubut) but its legal implication (dalalah) is zanni due to different interpretations.
4
Apply the Classification
Test your understanding by classifying additional examples, such as making up missed prayers, zakat, or Hajj. Determine which category each falls into and justify your reasoning.
Most ibadat are qadaa bi-mithl al-ma'qool, but some unique rulings like fidyah are irrational substitutes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والقضاء انواع أيضاً مثل معقول وبمثل غير معقول وماهو فى معنى الاداء كالصوم للصوم والفدية له وقضاء تكبيرات العيد في الركوع
تقسیم قضاء
قضاء کی بھی کئی قسمیں ہیں (1) قضاء بمثل المعقول .... یہ وہ قضاء ہے جس کی نہین کیسا تھ مماثلت عقل سے سمجھ آتی ہو شریعت سے قطع نظر کرتے ہوئے اس کی مثال جیسے روزے کی قضاء روزے سے کرنا ۔
(۲) قضا بمثل غیر معقول . یہ وہ قضاء ہے جس کی مین کیسا تھ مماثلت صرف شریعت سے سمجھ میں آتی ہو اور عقل اس کے سمجھنے سے قاصر ہو جیسے کہ روزے کی قضاء فدیہ سے۔ کیونکہ روزے اور فدیئے کے درمیان نہ تو صورۃ مماثلت پائی جاتی ہے اور نہ معنا کیونکہ روزے کا مقصد نفس کو بھوکا رکھنا ہے اور فدیے کا مقصد نفس کو سیر کرنا ہے مگر شریعت نے روزے کے قضاء میں قدینے کا حکم دیا ہے قرآن میں ہے وعلى الذين يطيقونه فدية اس کی کئی تفسیریں ہیں ۔ جن کو صاحب نورالانوار نے بیان کیا ہے۔
(۳) قضاء مشابہ بالا واہ یہ ہے کہ حقیقتا یا حاکما اس میں اداء کا معنی پایا جائے۔ قضاء ومشابہ بالا داء کی مثال یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص سے تکبیرات عیدین فوت ہو جائیں اور وہ امام
His saying: 'And qadaa also has several types: like with a rational equivalent, with an irrational equivalent, and what is in the meaning of performance, such as fasting for fasting, and fidyah for it, and the qadaa of the takbīrs of the two Eids in rukūʻ.' Division of qadaa: Qadaa also has several types: (1) Qadaa with a rational equivalent — this is qadaa whose similarity is understood by reason, regardless of Shariah. Example: making up fasting by fasting. (2) Qadaa with an irrational equivalent — this is qadaa whose similarity is understood only by Shariah, and reason is incapable of understanding it. Example: making up fasting by fidyah. Because between fasting and fidyah there is no formal similarity nor meaningful similarity, since the purpose of fasting is to keep the self hungry, and the purpose of fidyah is to satisfy the self, but Shariah has commanded fidyah for making up fasting. In the Qur'an it says: 'And upon those who are able, a fidyah [of feeding]' — there are several interpretations of this, which the author of Nūr al-Anwār has explained. (3) Qadaa resembling performance — this is that in which the meaning of performance is found, either literally or constructively. An example of qadaa resembling performance is if a person misses the takbīrs of the two Eids and then he [follows the imam?]
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage discusses the divisions of qadaa (making up missed obligations) from the perspective of usul al-fiqh. The author elaborates on the matn's classification into three categories based on the nature of the substitute offered.

- The first type is qadaa bi-mithl al-ma'qool: qadaa with a rational equivalent. Here, the substitute is clearly similar to the original in a way that reason can perceive without needing divine revelation. For example, making up a missed fast by fasting another day — the similarity is obvious.
- The second type is qadaa bi-mithl ghayr al-ma'qool: qadaa with an irrational equivalent. Reason alone cannot see the similarity; it is known only through Shariah. The example is making up a fast by paying fidyah (feeding the poor). Fasting and feeding are opposite in purpose (hunger vs. satiation), yet the Qur'an allows fidyah as a substitute for those able to fast but who choose not to (or for the elderly). The author notes that this verse has multiple interpretations, which the original matn (Nūr al-Anwār) explains further.
- The third type is qadaa mushabih bil-adaa: qadaa that resembles performance. In this case, the missed act is made up in a way that retains some element of the original performance. The example given (though the text cuts off) is about the takbīrs of Eid prayer that are missed and then performed in rukūʻ—this retains the adaa aspect because the takbīrs are performed within the same prayer, but it is still qadaa because they were missed at their proper time.

Key Terms
قضاء بمثل معقول (qadaa bi-mithl al-ma'qool): Qadaa where the substitute is rationally similar to the original obligation
قضاء بمثل غير معقول (qadaa bi-mithl ghayr al-ma'qool): Qadaa where the substitute's similarity is known only through Shariah, not reason
قضاء مشابہ بالاداء (qadaa mushabih bil-adaa): Qadaa that contains the meaning of adaa either literally or constructively

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of qadaa does the example of making up a missed fast by feeding the poor represent?
A) Qadaa bi-mithl al-ma'qool
B) Qadaa bi-mithl ghayr al-ma'qool
C) Qadaa mushabih bil-adaa
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of qadaa into rational, irrational, and performance-resembling types shows that Shariah can prescribe substitutes beyond what reason alone can analogize.

Compensation for Usurped Property: Mithl vs. Qima
Introducing qada types: guarantee of usurped property by like or by value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct form of compensation for a usurped mithli item, applying the Hanafi hierarchy: first mithl, then qima.

Core Idea
A usurper must compensate by like (mithl) if available; only when impossible does value (qima) suffice.

In Hanafi fiqh, the rights of servants (huquq al-ibad) include precise rules for compensation when property is wrongfully taken and destroyed. This lesson examines the principle of daman bi al-mithl — guaranteeing by a like — and the hierarchy that governs when replacement by value becomes acceptable. The passage from the matn and its commentary clarify this sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
You have a case of usurpation (ghasb) and destruction (halak) of an item. Determine if the item is mithli (fungible/measurable) or qimmi (unique). The passage focuses on mithli items.
Mithli items include grains, liquids, and other standardized goods.
2
Determine the primary obligation
The primary compensation is mithl (like) — an identical item in type and quantity. The text says it is wajib to give the mithl when possible.
This is based on the principle that the usurper must restore the status quo ante.
3
Understand the two types of 'mithl'
The commentator distinguishes 'mithl suri' (formal like: exact identical) and 'mithl ma'nawi' (meaningful like: value). Giving an exact identical is a complete difficulty, while giving value is a lesser difficulty.
These terms highlight the practical difficulty of obtaining an exact duplicate.
4
Apply the hierarchy
As long as a complete mithl (exact or similar enough) is available, we do not resort to the deficient mithl (qima). This establishes a clear priority.
The principle: al-baqi bi al-mithl muqaddam 'ala al-qima.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آگے صاحب کتاب اقسام قضاء کی مثالیں حقوق العباد کے اعتبار سے بیان فرمارہے ہیں ۔ اقسام قضاء کی حقوق العباد سے امثلہ قوله : ومنها ضمان المغصوب بالمثل وهو السابق او بالقيمة الخ قضاء بمثل معقول کی مثال : حقوق العباد میں قضا بمثل المعقول کی مثال یہ ہے کہ غاصب نے کوئی چیز غصب کرلی پھر اس کو ہلاک کردیا تو اگر وہ چیز ملی تھی تو غاصب پر اس کی مثل کی منمان دینا واجب ہوگا۔ لیکن مثل صوری کیسا تھ ضمان دینا یہ مشکل کامل ہے۔ اور مشکل معنوی یعنی قیمت کیسا تھ ضمان دینا یہ شل قاصر ہے تو جب تک مثل کامل پائی جاتی ہے اس وقت تک مثل قاصر کی طرف رجوع نہیں کریں گے۔
Now the author of the book is presenting examples of the types of qada' (compensation) with respect to the rights of servants. Examples of qada' from rights of servants. His saying: And from them is the guarantee of the usurped item by its like (mithl), which is the first, or by its value (qima), etc. An example of qada' by rational like: In the rights of servants, an example of qada' by an intelligible like is that a usurper took something, then destroyed it, so if that item was measurable (mithli), then it is wajib upon the usurper to give its like as compensation. But to give guarantee by formal like (mithl suri) is a complete difficulty. And to give guarantee by meaningful difficulty, i.e., by value, is a deficient difficulty. So as long as a complete mithl is available, we will not turn to the deficient mithl.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins a new section where the author provides examples of types of qada' (compensation) in the realm of huquq al-ibad (rights of servants/people). The text first mentions the ruling from the matn: among the examples is the guarantee (daman) of a usurped item (maghsoub) by its mithl (like/identical) or by its value (qima). The commentator explains that in huquq al-ibad, qada' by mithl ma'qul (intelligible like) applies when something measurable is usurped and destroyed. Then the passage distinguishes between two types of difficulty: 'mithl suri' (formal like, i.e., exactly identical) is a complete difficulty to guarantee, while 'mithl ma'nawi' (meaningful like, i.e., value) is a lesser difficulty. The principle is that as long as a complete mithl is available, one does not resort to a deficient one (like value). This establishes a hierarchy: first try to give the exact same item; if impossible, then give something similar in type and quality; only then give value.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Legal guarantee or liability to compensate for loss or damage.
مثل (mithl): An identical or similar item in kind and quantity, i.e., a like.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preferred form of compensation for a usurped mithli item according to the passage?
A) Its value (qima) in cash.
B) Its exact like (mithl) in quantity and quality.
C) Any substitute chosen by the usurper.
D) No compensation if the item is destroyed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, compensation for a usurped mithli item must first be by a like (mithl); only when a like is unavailable does value (qima) become acceptable.

Usufruct Not Guaranteed Upon Destruction: A Hanafi Usul Rule
Ruling with objection-answer on benefit guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that usufruct is not guaranteed upon destruction and distinguish it from consent-based contracts like ijarah.

Core Idea
Benefits perish as they occur; no guarantee attaches to what cannot be stored or valued.

This passage presents a foundational usul rule in the Hanafi school: the usufruct (manafi') of a property is not subject to guarantee when destroyed by unauthorized use. The reasoning turns on the nature of benefits as accidents (a'rad) that do not persist over time, unlike physical goods. An objection is raised: why then is compensation required in ijarah? The answer, based on mutual consent, clarifies the boundary between contractual and tortious liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Note that the ruling applies to any benefit (e.g., riding a horse, living in a house) destroyed by the usurper. The three main Hanafi imams agree on this point.
The ruling is unanimous among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Extract the underlying illa (effective cause)
The text states that benefits are accidents (a'rad) that do not persist over time. This is the key characteristic that prevents guarantee.
Compare with physical goods (mal) which persist and are valuable.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection analogizes to ijarah, where benefits are paid for. The response shows that consent transforms the basis of obligation: in contracts, parties voluntarily create liability.
This illustrates tarkhis al-illah (restriction of the cause) by a distinguishing factor.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider a usurper who lives in someone's house for a month without permission. According to this rule, he is not liable for the rental value of the month, though he must return the house intact.
Only physical damage to the house itself would be compensable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وقلنا جميعاً المنافع لا تضمن بالاتلاف (۱) پہلی تفریح اگر کسی شخص نے کسی کا گھوڑا غصب کر لیا اور پھر کچھ وقت اس پر سوارر ہایا سواری تو نہیں کی بلکہ اس کو گھر میں باندھا تو ہمارے تینوں علماء یعنی امام اعظم ابو حنیفہ اور ان کے مایہ ناز تلا ندہ امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد رحمہم اللہ کہتے ہیں کہ یہ منافع جو اس نے ہلاک کئے ہیں اس کی ضمان اس پر لازم نہیں ہوگی اب منافع کی منمان منافع کے ساتھ تو اس لئے نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ سوار سوار کے درمیان فرق ہوتا ہے۔ اور منافع کے درمیان کوئی مماثلت نہیں جسطرح اعیان میں کوئی مماثلت نہیں کیونکہ منافع عرض ہیں۔ روز مانوں تک باقی نہیں رہ سکتے اور یہ غیر متقوم ہے جب کہ مال جو ہر ہے اور دوز مانوں تک باقی رہ سکتا ہے اور متقوم بھی ہے اعتراض .... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ پھر اجارے کی صورت میں تم منافع کی ضمان کیوں واجب کرتے ہو۔ جواب .... وہاں متعاقدین کی رضامندی ہوتی ہے اور رضامندی سے اصل بھی واجب ہوتا ہے
He said: And we all said: Benefits (usufruct) are not guaranteed by destruction. (1) First derivation: If a person seizes someone's horse and then rides it for some time, or does not ride but ties it in the house, then our three scholars, i.e., Imam Abu Hanifa and his distinguished students Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, say that these benefits which he destroyed are not subject to guarantee. Now the guarantee of benefits will not be like the guarantee of benefits, because riding varies between riders, and there is no similarity between benefits just as there is no similarity between substance (entities). Because benefits are accidents (a'rad); they do not persist over time, and they are non-valuable, while wealth (mal) is substance and persists over time and is valuable. Objection: Then in the case of ijarah (hire), why do you make the guarantee of benefits obligatory? Answer: There, there is mutual consent of the contracting parties, and by consent even the original becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling that the usufruct (manafi') of a usurped item is not compensable upon destruction (itlaf). The three Hanafi imams agree that if someone usurps another's horse and uses it without permission, he is not liable for the value of the benefit. The reasoning is that benefits are accidents (a'rad) that do not persist and have no standardized value; they are not considered property (mal) in the same way as physical goods. An objection is raised: if that is the case, then why is compensation for benefits payable in a valid ijarah (hire) contract? The answer is that in ijarah, the parties consent to the transaction, and consent makes even the original obligatory (i.e., the contract itself creates liability). In usurpation, there is no consent, so no guarantee applies.

Key Terms
منافع (manafi'): Usufruct or benefits derived from a property, e.g., riding a horse, living in a house
عرض (arad): Accident, a non-permanent attribute
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or liability for compensation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the Hanafi imams rule that usufruct is not guaranteed in cases of usurpation?
A) Because usufruct is not property at all
B) Because usufruct are accidents that do not persist and have no similarity
C) Because the owner is not harmed
D) Because the usurper did not intend to cause loss

Key Takeaway
Benefits are accidents that perish with use; without consent, they carry no guarantee.

Guarantee When No Mithl Exists: The Case of Manafi in Ghasp
Establishing the principle of qimah when mithl is absent, applied to manafi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that when an item has no mithl (exact equivalent), its guarantee is by qimah (value), and to identify the reason manafi are not guaranteed in ghasp according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
If no mithl exists, qimah is due; manafi have no mithl, so no dhaman.

In this passage from *Khulasat al-Anwar*, the author establishes a key usul rule: compensation for a misappropriated asset is by returning the original, then its mithl (exact equivalent), and if mithl is impossible, then its value (qimah). This principle is then applied to the controversial case of manafi (benefits). The Hanafi school, unlike the Shafi'is, holds that manafi are not guaranteed in ghasp because they are a'rad (accidents) without persistence and without a mithl. We will unpack the rule, the evidence, and the cross-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy of dhaman
What are the three steps for compensating a misappropriated object? State them in order.
This hierarchy is the foundation of the rule.
2
Pinpoint the disagreement on timing
Find the difference between Imam Muhammad and the author on the valuation date. Which day is used? Why?
Imam Muhammad values at yawm al-intiqa', the author at yawm al-khusumah.
3
Extract the usul principle
The passage says: if a thing has no mithl at all, then its dhaman is wajib by what?
Answer: qimah (value).
4
Apply to the first tafri'ah: manafi
How does the author apply the principle to manafi? Why are manafi not guaranteed?
Because manafi are a'rad, non-mutaqawwim, and have no mithl.
5
Analyze the objection and response
The objection: why are manafi guaranteed in ijarah? The response distinguishes based on consent. How does consent change the ruling?
In ijarah there is consent; in ghasp there is none.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القیم میں حکم یہ تھا کہ اصل کو واپس کرے اگر اصل واپس ہو سکے اگر اصل واپس نہیں کر سکتا تو مثل اپس کرے اگر مثل سے بھی عاجز ہو تو پھر قیمت ہی واجب ہوگی۔
(۳) امام محمد کامذہب امام محمد کے نزدیک یوم الانقطاع کی قیمت کا اعتبار ہوگا کیونکہ اصل کے دینے سے عاجز ہونا اسی دن ثابت ہو گا۔
امام محمد کی دلیل کارو... ہم کہتے ہیں عاجز ہونا تو اس دن ثابت ہو گا مگر عجز ظاہر اس وقت ہوگا جب قاضی کے سامنے مقدمہ پیش ہو گا لہذا خصومت والے روز کا ہی اعتبار کیا جائے گا۔ یہاں سے ایک اور اصول سمجھ میں آتا ہے وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی چیز کی مثل نہ ہو نہ مشکل کامل، اور نہ مثل قاصر، اور نہ مثل صوری اور نہ مشکل معنوی تو اس کا ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ جب مثل معدوم ہو تو منمان واجب ہوگا۔
اس پر مصنف نے تین تفریعات اٹھا ئیں ہیں۔
قوله : وقلنا جميعاً المنافع لا تضمن بالاتلاف (۱) پہلی تفریح اگر کسی شخص نے کسی کا گھوڑا غصب کر لیا اور پھر کچھ وقت اس پر سوارر ہایا سواری تو نہیں کی بلکہ اس کو گھر میں باندھا تو ہمارے تینوں علماء یعنی امام اعظم ابو حنیفہ اور ان کے مایہ ناز تلا ندہ امام ابو یوسف اور امام محمد رحمہم اللہ کہتے ہیں کہ یہ منافع جو اس نے ہلاک کئے ہیں اس کی ضمان اس پر لازم نہیں ہوگی اب منافع کی منمان منافع کے ساتھ تو اس لئے نہیں ہوگی۔ کیونکہ سوار سوار کے درمیان فرق ہوتا ہے۔ اور منافع کے درمیان کوئی مماثلت نہیں جسطرح اعیان میں کوئی مماثلت نہیں کیونکہ منافع عرض ہیں۔ روز مانوں تک باقی نہیں رہ سکتے اور یہ غیر متقوم ہے جب کہ مال جو ہر ہے اور دوز مانوں تک باقی رہ سکتا ہے اور متقوم بھی ہے
اعتراض .... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ پھر اجارے کی صورت میں تم منافع کی ضمان کیوں واجب کر تے ہو۔
:
جواب .... وہاں متعاقدین کی رضامندی ہوتی ہے اور رضامندی سے اصل بھی واجب ہوتا ہے اور زائد بھی واجب ہوتا ہے لیکن غصب کی صورت میں رضا مندی نہیں ہے۔ لیکن امام شافعی کے نزدیک عرف کے مطابق منافع کا ضمان واجب ہو گا
(1)
پھر یہاں تین چیزیں ہیں:
مغصوب نفسہ اس کی ضمان ہلاک اور استھلاک دونوں سے واجب ہوگی
(۲) زوائد جیسے جانور کا دودھ اور بچے وغیرہ ان کی ضمان نہ ہلاک سے آئے گی اور نہ افلاک سے آئیگی جیسے گھوڑے پر سواری کرنا۔
(۳) منافع ... احناف کے ہاں ضمان نہیں ہے اور شوافع کے ہاں ضمان واجب ہے احناف کے ہاں بلاک کی صورت میں جیسے ماتن نے تلف سے تعبیر کیا ہے اور نہ استعملاک کی صورت میں جسے ماتن : اتلاف سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔
The ruling on the value was that the original should be returned if possible; if not, then its like; if unable even with that, then the value becomes obligatory. (3) The madhhab of Imam Muhammad: According to Imam Muhammad, the value on the day of inability (yawm al-intiqa') will be considered because inability to return the original is proven on that day. Argument of Imam Muhammad: ... We say: Inability is proven on that day, but the apparent inability occurs when the case is presented before the judge; hence the day of litigation is considered. From here another principle is understood: that if a thing has no mithl — neither a difficult complete mithl, nor a defective mithl, nor a formal mithl, nor a difficult conceptual mithl — then its dhaman is obligatory. When mithl is non-existent, then value is obligatory. The author derives three furu' from this. He said: 'And we all say: benefits (manafi) are not guaranteed by destruction.' First branch: If a person misappropriated someone's horse and tied it up without riding, our three imams — Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad — say that the benefits he destroyed are not subject to dhaman. Now the guarantee of manafi is not because there is no similarity between one benefit and another, unlike physical objects. Because manafi are accidents ('arad), they do not persist over time, they are not property (ghayr mutaqawwim), while physical property persists and is mutaqawwim. Objection: Then why in the case of ijarah do you make guarantee of manafi wajib? Answer: There, mutual consent is present, and with consent both original and increase become obligatory, but in ghasp there is no consent. However, according to Imam al-Shafi'i, based on custom, the guarantee of manafi is wajib. Then there are three things: (1) The misappropriated object itself: its dhaman is obligatory from both destruction and consumption. (2) Increases, like milk and offspring: their dhaman is not obligatory from destruction nor from consumption like riding. (3) Manafi: According to Hanafis, no dhaman; according to Shafi'is, dhaman is wajib. According to Hanafis, in the case of destruction (talaf) as expressed by the matn, and not in the case of consumption (istihlak) which the matn calls itlaf.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the usul al-fiqh commentary explaining the principle of dhaman for things without a mithl. The author begins by summarizing the general rule: in cases of misappropriation, the original property must be returned; if that is impossible, then its mithl (exact equivalent); if mithl is impossible, then its value (qimah) is due. He then discusses the disagreement between Imam Muhammad and the author regarding the valuation date: Imam Muhammad considers the day of inability (yawm al-intiqa'), while the author says it is the day of litigation because inability becomes apparent only then. This leads to a broader usul principle: if an item has no mithl at all — meaning no exact equivalent exists in kind — then only qimah is due. The author then applies this principle to three cases (tafri'at). The first tafri'ah is about manafi (benefits) in ghasp: the Hanafi school unanimously holds that the mere destruction of potential benefits (e.g., not riding a misappropriated horse) does not entail dhaman because benefits are not property (ghayr mutaqawwim) and do not persist; they are accidents that cannot be exactly replicated. An objection is raised: why is dhaman of manafi wajib in ijarah? The answer: ijarah is based on mutual consent, which makes the benefit a contractual obligation, whereas ghasp lacks consent. The passage also contrasts with Shafi'i view, which based on custom considers manafi as compensable. Finally, three categories are distinguished: the misappropriated object itself (always guaranteed), increases (never guaranteed in ghasp), and manafi (Hanafi: no; Shafi'i: yes).

Key Terms
منافع (manafi): Benefits derived from an object, like riding a horse; considered arad (accidents) that do not persist
مثل (mithl): An exact equivalent in kind; if available, it must be returned; if not, then value
ضمان (dhaman): Legal liability to compensate for loss
عرض (arad): An accident or non-permanent attribute of a substance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why are manafi (benefits) not guaranteed in a case of ghasp?
A) Because the misappropriator had no intention to use them.
B) Because manafi are a'rad (accidents) that do not persist and have no mithl.
C) Because the owner was negligent.
D) Because the object was returned unharmed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's position that manafi are not guaranteed in ghasp rests on the usul principle that only property with a mithl or substitutable value is compensable; manafi are neither.

The Principle: Qisas Has No Mithl – No Liability for the Killer's Killer
Applying the no-mithl rule to qisas branch

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that qisas is not property, identify its implication when a third party kills the murderer, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection based on accidental killing analogy.

Core Idea
Qisas is not property – killing the killer does not trigger compensation because it has no exact equivalent.

This lesson confronts a subtle branch of the rule 'what has no mithl cannot be guaranteed.' The author applies it to qisas: if a stranger kills the murderer, no liability falls on him for the lost qisas, even though he may be liable for the new homicide. This challenges the instinct that 'a right lost implies compensation.' We will deconstruct the Hanafi reasoning and why the Shafi'i school sees it differently.

Guided Path
1
State the core Hanafi principle
Identify the general rule: 'Ma la mithl lahu la yudman' – something without an exact equivalent cannot be guaranteed. This is a usul rule governing damages.
This rule also applies to hadd punishments and other non-property rights.
2
Identify the branch (tafri'ah) in the text
Recognize that the passage presents the second branch (tafri'ah) of this rule: qisas has no mithl, so if a third party kills the murderer, no dhaman for qisas arises. The heirs of the original victim cannot claim qisas from the third party.
The third party may still be liable for killing the murderer (second victim), but not for the lost qisas.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
State the Shafi'i view: the third party is liable because diyah is wajib in accidental killing, suggesting qisas has value. Understand their qiyas: intentional killing (qisas) is analogous to accidental killing (diyah).
The Shafi'i rely on the common element of life-taking to infer monetary equivalence.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Unpack the Hanafi rebuttal: diyah is a policy (siyasah) to preserve life, not a valuation of qisas. Moreover, the third party actually benefits the heirs by eliminating their enemy – no loss incurred.
The Hanafi distinguish between qisas (personal retaliation) and diyah (blood money) as separate legal institutions.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi says no compensation from the third party for the lost qisas; Shafi'i says the third party must pay the value of qisas to the heirs.
This affects inheritance and criminal liability calculations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والقصاص لا يضمن بقتل القاتل
مصنف نے جو اصول بیان کیا کہ جس چیز کی مثل نہیں اس کی ضمان بھی نہیں اس پر دوسری تفریح یہ اٹھاتے ہیں کہ اگر قاتل کو کسی اجنبی شخص نے قتل کر دیا تو مقتول اول کے ورثاء اس قاتل سے مہمان کا مطالبہ نہیں کریں گے اگر چہ مقتول ثانی کے ورثاء اس اجنبی سے ضمان کا مطالبہ کر سکتے ہیں۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ قصاص کوئی متقوم چیز نہیں اور اس کی کوئی مثل بھی نہیں لہذا قصاص کے ضائع ہونے سے اجنبی پر کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں آئیگا ۔ امام شافعی کا مذہب ... امام شافع کے نزدیک یہ اجنبی ضامن ہوگا ۔ اس لئے سر قتل خطا میں دیت واجب ہوتی ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ قصاص متقوم چیز ہے۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل کارو ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ قتل خطاء کے لئے کوئی مثل نہیں تا ہم مسلمان کے خون کو صدرہونے سے بچانے کیلئے ہم نے دیت کو واجب کیا لیکن یہاں پر اجنبی نے مقتول کے ورثاء کا کوئی نقصان نہیں کیا بلکہ ان کے دشمن کو قتل کر کے ان کی مدد کی ہے لہذا ضمان واجب نہیں ہوگا۔
He said: 'And qisas is not guaranteed by killing the killer.' The author, upon the principle he stated that something without a mithl is not guaranteed, raises a second branch: if a stranger kills the murderer, the heirs of the first victim will not demand qisas from this killer, although the heirs of the second victim can demand dhaman from the stranger. The reason is that qisas is not a property (mutaqawwim) and has no mithl; therefore, the stranger incurs no dhaman for the loss of qisas. The madhhab of Imam al-Shafi'i: according to him, the stranger is damin (liable) because in the case of accidental killing, diyah is wajib, showing that qisas is mutaqawwim. Argument of Imam al-Shafi'i: ... We reply: For accidental killing there is no mithl, but we made diyah wajib to prevent the waste of a Muslim's blood; however, here the stranger did not harm the heirs — rather, he helped them by killing their enemy — so no dhaman is wajib.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a ruling presenting the second application (tafri'ah) of the principle that items without a mithl are not subject to dhaman. The matn states: 'And qisas is not guaranteed by killing the killer.' The sharh explains: if a third party kills a murderer, the original victim's heirs cannot demand qisas from this third party because qisas is not a property right (ghayr mutaqawwim) and has no exact equivalent (mithl). The third party may be liable to the second victim's heirs for homicide, but not for the lost qisas. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, analogizing from accidental killing where diyah is imposed, implying qisas has value. The Hanafi response distinguishes: in accidental killing, diyah is a mercy to prevent bloodshed, not because qisas is property. Moreover, the third party actually benefits the heirs by executing their enemy, so no compensation is due.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retributive punishment of death for intentional killing
دیت (diyah): Blood money paid for accidental killing
مثل (mithl): Exact equivalent or counterpart, necessary for dhaman (guarantee)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a third party not liable for the lost qisas when they kill the murderer?
A) Because the third party acted in self-defence.
B) Because qisas is not mutaqawwim and has no mithl.
C) Because the heirs already received diyah.
D) Because the third party had official permission.

Key Takeaway
Qisas has no mithl, so its loss by a third party killing the murderer incurs no dhaman under Hanafi law.

The Four Divisions of Obligation Attachment in Time-Muqayyad Commands
Explaining the four divisions of obligation attachment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which part of time the obligation attaches to in each scenario of performing a time-restricted act, and apply this classification to determine whether an act is ada or qada.

Core Idea
Obligation attaches to the first moment, the performed moment, the last moment, or the whole window.

This lesson explores a fundamental taxonomy in Hanafi usul: when a command is restricted to a time (amr muqayyad bi al-waqt), how does the obligation relate to that time? The answer determines whether an act is considered timely (ada) or missed (qada). We will examine the four possible attachments as explained in the source.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of time-muqayyad command
A command like 'Pray the Zuhr prayer' is restricted by time. The obligation to pray exists within a specific time window. How does the shari'ah assign the obligation to the parts of that window?
This is the context for the four divisions.
2
Identify the four possible parts of time
The classification uses four parts: (1) the first part (juz' awwal), (2) the connected part (juz' muttasil) which is the actual part when one prays, (3) the deficient part (juz' naqis) at the end sufficient only for takbir, and (4) the entire time (jumlat al-waqt).
The deficient part is specifically defined as the minimal part to perform the act in Hanafi school.
3
Map each scenario to the corresponding attachment
Four scenarios: praying at the beginning (attachment to juz' awwal), praying later within the valid time (attachment to juz' muttasil), reaching the time when only a deficient part remains (attachment to juz' naqis), and missing the entire time (attachment to jumlat al-waqt).
The third scenario applies to 'Asr prayer when the sun begins to set.
4
Apply the classification to determine ada vs qada
If the obligation attaches to any part within the time (first, connected, or deficient), the prayer is considered ada (timely). If it attaches to the entire time due to complete failure, it becomes qada.
This has practical implications for rulings on missing parts of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وهو اما أن يضاف الى الجزء الأول أو الى مايلي : ابتداء الشروع أو الى الجزء الناقص عند ضيق الوقت أو الى جملة الوقت.
امر مقید کی تقسیم
امر مقید کی اس پہلی قسم کی چار قسمیں ہیں۔
وجہ صر .... امر مقید بالوقت کی قسم اول کے اندر وجوب کی اضافت یا تو وقت کے جزء اول کیطرف ہوگی یا نماز کے وقت شروع کی طرف ہوگی یا جزء ناقص کی طرف
ہوگی یا کل وقت کی طرف ہوگی۔
(1)
کی طرف ہوگی
پہلی قسم یہ ہے کہ اگر کوئی شخص اول وقت میں نماز ادا کرے تو وجوب کی نسبت جز اول
(۲) دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ اگر بعد میںکسی صحیح وقت میں ادا کرے تو وجوب کی نسبت جز متصل
کی طرف ہوگی۔
(۳) تیسری قسم یہ ہے کہ اگر صحیح وقت میں ادا نہ کر سکے تو وجوب کی نسبت جزء ناقص کی طرف
ہوگی جیسا کہ نماز عصر میں ہوتا ہے۔
(۴) چوتھی قسم یہ ہے کہ اگر وقت کے اندر بالکل ادا نہ کر سکے اور نماز قضاء ہو جائے تو وجوب کی نسبت سارے وقت کی طرف ہوگی۔
He said: And it is either added to the first part or to what follows the beginning of entering into prayer or to the deficient part at the constriction of time or to the entire time. Division of the muqayyad command: This first type of the muqayyad command has four divisions. According to reason (?)... In the first type of time-muqayyad command, the addition of obligation is either towards the first part of time or towards the beginning of prayer time or towards the deficient part or towards the entire time. (1) First division: if a person prays at the first time, then the obligation is related to the first part. (2) Second division: if he prays later in a valid time, then the obligation is related to the connected part. (3) Third division: if he cannot pray in the valid time, then the obligation is related to the deficient part, like in 'Asr prayer. (4) Fourth division: if he cannot pray within the time at all and the prayer is made up, then the obligation is related to the entire time.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the four ways in which the obligation (wujub) of a time-bound act, such as prayer, is attached to the time. The author cites the earlier text (qalahu) and then explains it. The muqayyad command (amr muqayyad) here refers to commands restricted by time. The first type of such commands concerns how the obligation is assigned to the time interval. The four divisions are: (1) If one prays at the very beginning, the obligation is attributed to the first part of time. (2) If one prays later but within the valid time, the obligation is attributed to the part of time actually used for prayer (the 'connected part'). (3) If one delays until only a deficient part remains (enough for opening takbir), the obligation is attributed to that deficient part, as in 'Asr. (4) If one fails to pray entirely and must make up (qada), the obligation is attributed to the whole time. This classification is crucial for determining when a prayer is considered ada (timely) vs. qada, and the effects of nullification.

Key Terms
جزء ناقص (juz' naqis): The deficient part of time at the end of the prayer window, sufficient only for the opening takbir.
جزء متصل (juz' muttasil): The connected part of time that is actually used for prayer when performed later in the valid time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person prays at the very beginning of the time, to which part of time is the obligation attached?
A) First part (juz' awwal)
B) Connected part (juz' muttasil)
C) Deficient part (juz' naqis)
D) Entire time (jumlat al-waqt)

Key Takeaway
The obligation of a time-restricted act can attach to the first, connected, deficient part, or entire time, determining whether the act is ada or qada.

The Qada of a Missed Prayer Must Be in a Complete Time
Stating a deduced ruling from the previous principle about complete and deficient time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the usul principle that the deficiency of time for ada does not transfer to qada of a missed prayer.

Core Idea
The obligation of qada mirrors the original: complete time requires complete prayer.

This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Anwar illustrates a key usul al-fiqh principle: the time in which a prayer is performed (ada) may be deficient, but when making up a missed prayer (qada), the original complete time governs the obligation. The commentator shows why yesterday's 'Asr cannot be performed in the deficient part of today's 'Asr time, while today's 'Asr itself can be. This builds on earlier distinctions between complete and deficient time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its time classification
Identify the two prayers: yesterday's 'Asr (qada) and today's 'Asr (ada). Note that today's 'Asr time has a deficient part (the end of its time).
2
Determine the cause of obligation
The obligation of yesterday's 'Asr was tied to the complete time of yesterday. That complete time is the sabab (cause) of the obligation.
The sabab is what triggers the obligation; here it's the complete time period.
3
Apply the rule: qada replicates the cause
Since the original obligation was based on complete time, the qada must also be performed in a complete time, not in the deficient part of today.
The deficient time only suffices for the ada of today's 'Asr because today's obligation is directly linked to that time.
4
Differentiate ada from qada
Today's 'Asr (ada) can be performed in the deficient time because its obligation is directly linked to that time period. Yesterday's 'Asr cannot because its cause was the complete time of yesterday.
5
State the usul principle
The ruling shows that when the cause of obligation (sabab) is a complete time, the qada requires a complete time; deficient time only suffices for ada when the original obligation was tied to that incomplete period.
This principle applies to all prayers, not just 'Asr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فلهذا لايتادى عصر امسه في الوقت الناقص بخلاف عصر يومه
تفریح ... جب نماز وقت میں ادا نہ کر سکے تو پورا وقت وجوب کا سبب ہوگا اور پورا وقت تو کامل ہے اس پر کامل نما ز ہی واجب ہوگی لہذا کل گذشتہ کی عصر کی نماز وقت ناقص میں ادا نہیں ہوسکتی کیو نکہ جب اس کا سب کامل وقت ہے تو اس کی قضہ ، کاش وقت ہی میں کرے گا لیکن آج کی عصر کی
He said: For this reason, the 'Asr of yesterday is not performed in the deficient time, contrary to the 'Asr of today. Branching: When a prayer cannot be performed in its time, the entire time becomes the cause of obligation, and the entire time is complete, so for it the complete prayer is obligatory. Hence, the 'Asr prayer of yesterday cannot be performed in the deficient time because that prayer's cause is the complete time, so one would perform its qada in the [complete] time. But today's 'Asr...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific fiqh ruling: The 'Asr prayer that was missed yesterday (qada) cannot be performed in the deficient part of today's 'Asr time. Why? Because the obligation for yesterday's 'Asr was rooted in the entire complete time of that day. When making it up (qada), one must do so in a complete time, not in the deficient end of today's time. The deficient time only suffices for the current day's 'Asr (ada), because that prayer's obligation was tied to the deficient part. This illustrates the practical application of the earlier distinctions between complete and deficient time. The commentary uses 'فلهذا' to indicate a deduced ruling from the previous principle.

Key Terms
عصر امسہ ('asr amsihi): The 'Asr prayer of yesterday (the previous day).
وقت ناقص (waqt naqis): Deficient time: the latter part of a prayer's time slot where the prayer is still valid but the time is incomplete in terms of optimality or legal cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only for today's 'Asr

Key Takeaway
The obligation of qada mirrors the original cause: a prayer missed in complete time must be made up in complete time.

Disbelievers as Addressees of Shariah Commands
Explaining that disbelievers are addressed by the commands of iman, hudud, and muamalat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that disbelievers are addressed by commands of faith, penalties, and transactions, and explain the rationale for each category.

Core Idea
Disbelievers are bound by Islamic law for worldly order—Hudud are deterrents, not expiations.

In Hanafi usul, the question of whether disbelievers are addressed by Shari'ah commands (mukhataboon bil-furu') is a key principle. This lesson unpacks the rule that they are indeed addressed with iman, hudud, and muamalat, and explains the underlying reasoning, including Imam Abu Hanifa's unique view that hudud are purely deterrents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Categories
From the passage, extract the three types of commands addressed to disbelievers: faith (iman), penal laws (uqubat), and transactions (muamalat).
2
Examine the Rationale for Faith
The command to believe is primarily directed at disbelievers; the verse 'O you who believe, believe' is for confirmation and increase.
3
Analyze the Rationale for Penalties
Penalties are applied to maintain worldly order and deter sin. Imam Abu Hanifa specifically holds that hudud and qisas are zawajir (deterrents), not expiations for the afterlife.
This distinguishes the Hanafi view from others who might see them as expiatory.
4
Understand the Rationale for Transactions
Since Muslims and disbelievers engage in mutual dealings, the same transactional rules apply to maintain social harmony.
5
Connect to the Usul Principle
Recognize that the Hanafi school holds disbelievers to be mukhataboon (addressees) of both usul (fundamentals) and furu' (branches) for the purpose of worldly accountability.
This has implications for enforcement of hadud and validity of contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والكفار مخاطبون بالامر بالايمان وبالمشروع من العقوبات والمعاملات . کفار کن اوامر کے مخاطب ہیں؟

کفار ایمان عقوبات اور معاملات کے مخاطب ہیں ایمان کے مخاطب تو اسلئے ہیں کہ ایمان کا حکم حقیقت میں کافروں کو ہوتا ہے جہاں تک (یا ایها الذین آمنوا امنوا) کا تعلق ہے تو یہاں امر بالایمان ثبات اور زیادتی کیلئے ہے۔ اور عقوبات کے مخاطب اس لئے ہیں کہ جہان کے نظام کو درست رکھنے کے لئے اور لوگوں کو گناہوں سے روکنے کیلئے جیسے مسلمانوں پر حد و قصاص جاری ہوتے ہیں اسی طرح کافروں پر بھی جاری ہونگے خاص طور پر امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس لئے کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک حدود و قصاص وغیرہ زواجر ہیں ساتھ نہیں ہے یعنی ان کے اجراء سے آخرت میں رفع درجات نہیں ہوگا ۔

اس طرح وہ معاملات کے بھی مخاطب ہیں اس لئے کہ ہمارے اور ان کے درمیان معاملات ہوتے ہیں تو جیسے مسلمان آپس میں معاملات کرتے ہیں ایسے ہی کافروں کے ساتھ بھی کرتے ہیں۔
He said: And the disbelievers are addressed by the command of faith and by the prescribed laws of penalties and transactions. Which commands are the disbelievers addressed by? The disbelievers are addressed by (commands of) faith, penalties, and transactions. They are addressed by faith because the command of faith in reality is directed to the disbelievers. As for the verse 'O you who believe, believe', the command to faith here is for confirmation and increase. And they are addressed by penalties because to maintain the system of the world and to prevent people from sins, just as hudud and qisas are implemented upon Muslims, similarly they will be implemented upon disbelievers, especially according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because in his view, hudud and qisas etc. are deterrents (zawajir), not expiations (kaffarat); that is, their implementation does not lead to elevation of ranks in the afterlife. Similarly, they are addressed by transactions because transactions occur between us and them; so just as Muslims conduct transactions among themselves, they also conduct with disbelievers.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the commands of Shariah are not limited to Muslims; disbelievers are also addressed by three categories: the command to believe (iman), penal laws (uqubat), and transactional laws (muamalat). The author notes that the command to believe is primarily directed at them, as in the verse 'O you who believe, believe...' which actually calls for strengthening faith. For penal laws, the reasoning is societal order and deterrence. Imam Abu Hanifa specifically holds that hudud and qisas are purely deterrent (zawajir) and not expiatory (kaffarat) for the afterlife, meaning that their enforcement does not benefit the disbeliever in the hereafter but serves worldly justice. Transactions with disbelievers are valid and governed by the same principles as between Muslims, since they are part of society.

Key Terms
الكفار (al-kuffar): Disbelievers; those who reject Islam.
مخاطبون (mukhataboon): Addressees of the divine commands.
زواجر (zawajir): Deterrent penalties that serve as worldly punishment, not expiation for the hereafter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To expiate their sins and grant them rewards in the afterlife.
B) To maintain the system of the world and deter people from sins.
C) Only if they have accepted Islam and then apostatized.
D) Because they are considered Muslims in legal matters.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul holds that disbelievers are addressed by commands of iman, uqubat, and muamalat for worldly order and accountability.

6 min
Partial Validity of a Sale When One Item Perishes Before Delivery
Hanafi ruling on sale of two slaves when one dies before delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of partial enforceability (bāʿ bi al-ḥiṣṣah) to cases where one of multiple items sold under a single contract is destroyed before delivery.

Core Idea
A single contract can be partially valid: if one item perishes, the sale of the surviving item remains enforceable.

This lesson presents a classical Hanafi ruling on the partial enforceability of a sale contract. The scenario involves two slaves sold together without separate prices. When one dies before delivery, the contract is not entirely void but remains valid for the living slave. This ruling illustrates a key legal principle: a contract can be split (bāʿ bi al-ḥiṣṣah), and what remains is still a valid sale. Understanding this principle is essential for applying Hanafi law to modern multi-item transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
Two items are sold together in one contract without individual prices.
The absence of separate prices is a key condition.
2
Determine the intervening event
One of the items perishes before delivery to the buyer.
The death of a slave is the specific event here.
3
State the Hanafi ruling
The sale of the dead item is void; the sale of the living item remains valid.
This is the explicit ruling from the passage.
4
Understand the legal basis
The contract can be partially enforced (bāʿ bi al-ḥiṣṣah), so the part that can still be performed is upheld.
This principle is stated in the passage as 'بیع بالحصہ بقاء'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر کسی نے دو غلام فروخت کئے اور ان کی علیحدہ علیحدہ قیمت بیان نہیں کی اور ان میں سے ایک غلام مشتری کے حوالے کرنے سے پہلے مرگیا تو گویا کہ اس کی بیع منسوخ ہوگئی اور جو زندہ ہے اس کی بیچ باقی رہے گی اور چونکہ یہ بیع بالحصہ بقاء ہے لہذا جائز ہے۔
If someone sold two slaves and did not state their separate prices, and one of the slaves died before delivery to the buyer, then it is as if the sale of that one is void, and the sale of the living one remains. And since this sale is partial in existence, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This is a Hanafi fiqh ruling regarding a sale that becomes partial due to the death of one item before delivery. According to Hanafi law, when a person sells two items in one contract without specifying individual prices, the contract is valid for the whole. However, if one item perishes before delivery, the contract is annulled for that item but remains valid for the other. The reasoning is that the contract can be split (باء بالحصہ) and what remains is still a viable sale. This is a practical application of the principle that a single contract can be partially valid.

Key Terms
بیع بالحصہ بقاء (bayʿ bi al-ḥiṣṣah baqāʾ): Partial sale: a sale that is valid only for the portion that is still possible to deliver.
مشتری (mushtarī): Buyer or purchaser.
حوالے کرنا (hawālah karnā): To deliver or hand over possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the sale of the living slave when one of two slaves dies before delivery in the Hanafi ruling?
A) The sale is void.
B) The sale remains valid.
C) The buyer can choose.
D) The seller must refund.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a contract can be partially valid: if one item perishes before delivery, the sale remains enforceable for the surviving item.

The Rule of Noun Repetition: Definite to Indefinite
Rule on noun repetition and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that when a definite noun is repeated as indefinite, the second is generally a different entity, and identify the exceptions where this rule does not hold.

Core Idea
When a definite noun is repeated as indefinite, the second is typically different—but this is a majority rule, not absolute.

This passage from Nur al-Anwar explains a critical usul al-fiqh principle regarding how repeated nouns relate to each other. It begins by reinforcing the interpretation of a key Quranic verse about hardship and ease, supported by a hadith, then lays out the general rule: a definite noun repeated as indefinite refers to a different entity. However, the author immediately qualifies this as a majority rule (qa'idah akthariyyah) and provides several Quranic examples that break the pattern. Understanding this nuance prevents rigid application and reflects the depth of Hanafi textual analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
From the hadith and fiqh example, extract the rule: when a definite noun is repeated as an indefinite noun, the second mention refers to a different entity than the first.
The example of 'yusr' repeated as 'yusrain' supports this.
2
Examine the dalil (proof)
Analyze the hadith 'Lan yaghliba 'usrun yusrain' — its wording shows two distinct 'yusr' with one 'usr', indicating that the indefinite repetition creates a new referent.
The dalil_block in the analytical toolbox details this.
3
Understand the fiqh illustration
Apply the rule to the debt acknowledgement example: a specific thousand (definite) followed by an absolute thousand (indefinite) creates two separate debts, confirming the rule.
This links usul to practical fiqh.
4
Recognize the exceptions
Identify the four Quranic exceptions provided: (1) definite repeated as definite but different referents, (2) indefinite repeated as indefinite but same referent, (3) definite repeated as definite but same referent, (4) definite repeated as indefinite but same referent. Each is supported by a verse.
These show the rule is not universal.
5
Internalize the concept of 'qa'idah akthariyyah'
Understand that the author explicitly calls this a majority rule, meaning the exceptions are acknowledged but the default remains operative unless contravened by evidence.
This is a crucial nuance in Hanafi usul reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is brought, therefore it is not the first. So it is known that with one difficulty there are two easements. This same meaning is known from the hadith of Abdullah ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) 'Lan yaghliba 'usrun yusrain' and from the poet's verse: 'When hardship befalls you, reflect on the verse 'Alam nashrah'; for there is ease between two difficulties when you reflect, so rejoice.' And when a definite noun is brought again as indefinite, the second is not the first, because if it were the same first, it would have been specified without any particle, whereas reflection does not specify. An example: if a person first acknowledges a thousand that is documented, then he acknowledges an absolute thousand in another session, the second is not the first, and two thousand become due. What has been mentioned above occurs at the time of application. In short, it is not a universal rule but a majority rule. Otherwise, it can also happen that a noun is brought again as definite but it is not the first, such as the statement of al-Tadarruj al-'Izzah? 'Hadha kitabun anzalnahu...' the first book refers to the Quran and the second book to the Torah and Injil. Also, it can happen that an indefinite is brought again as indefinite but it is the first instead of not the first, such as Allah's statement 'Wa huwa alladhi fi al-sama'i Allah wa fi al-ardi Allah'. Similarly, it can happen that a definite is brought again as definite but it is not the first, such as in the Quran 'Wa huwa alladhi anzala 'alayka al-kitab', the first book refers to the Holy Quran and the second to the Torah etc. And it can also happen that a definite is brought again as indefinite but it is the first, such as in the Quran 'Innama ilahukum Allahu wahid'. After this, the author says: the extent to which specification reaches, its two divisions are as follows.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage discusses the usul principle regarding the repetition of definite and indefinite nouns. The author begins by explaining that when the word 'usrun' (difficulty) is mentioned in the context of the Quranic verse 'fa inna ma'a al-'usri yusran', the second mention of 'yusr' is different from the first, because it is repeated as indefinite. This is supported by the hadith of Ibn Abbas 'Lan yaghliba 'usrun yusrayn' and a poetic line. The general rule is that when a definite noun is repeated as indefinite, it refers to a different entity. The author gives a fiqh example: if someone first acknowledges a debt of a specific thousand (documented) and later acknowledges an absolute thousand in another session, they are separate debts and the person owes two thousand. However, this rule is not universal but a majority rule (qa'idah akthariyyah). The author then provides exceptions: sometimes a repeated definite still refers to the same entity, as in 'hada kitabun anzalnahu' where the first 'kitab' is the Quran and the second is the Torah and Injil (different). Actually, that example shows different. Also, a repeated indefinite can refer to the same, as in 'Wa huwa alladhi fi al-sama'i Allah wa fi al-ardi Allah'. A repeated definite can refer to different entities, as in 'Wa huwa alladhi anzala 'alayka al-kitab' where the first is the Quran and the second is previous scriptures. Finally, a definite repeated as indefinite can refer to the same, as in 'Innama ilahukum Allahu wahid'. This demonstrates nuanced application. The lesson concludes by transitioning to the next topic.

Key Terms
نکرہ (nakirah): An indefinite noun that does not specify a particular entity.
معرفہ (ma‘rifah): A definite noun that specifies a particular entity or class.
قاعدہ اکثریہ (qa'idah akthariyyah): A majority rule, applying in most cases but subject to exceptions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They always refer to the same entity.
B) They always refer to different entities.
C) The second is usually different from the first, but exceptions exist.
D) The first is different from the second.

Key Takeaway
The repetition of a definite noun as indefinite generally creates a new referent, but this is a majority rule, not absolute, as demonstrated by Quranic exceptions.

Two Types of Mutashabihat
Classifying mutashabihat into two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mutashabih al-ma'na from mutashabih al-murad and correctly classify examples of each.

Core Idea
Mutashabihat divide into two: meaning unknown, and meaning known but Allah's intent unknown.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi classification of ambiguous Quranic verses (mutashabihat) into two categories. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how the Hanafi school approaches verses that are not immediately clear, whether in lexical meaning or divine intent. The passage provides concise definitions and examples that anchor this foundational concept.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the definition of mutashabih
Mutashabih refers to Quranic verses that are not entirely clear (muhkam). They require special interpretive effort.
2
Distinguish mutashabih al-ma'na
This type has a meaning that is completely unknowable to humans. No linguistic analysis can reveal it.
Example: al-huruf al-muqatta'at (disjointed letters).
3
Distinguish mutashabih al-murad
Here the lexical meaning is known (e.g., 'hand', 'face'), but the exact divine intent is unknown because Allah is not a body.
Example: yad Allah, wajh Allah.
4
Apply the classification
When encountering a potentially ambiguous verse, first ask: Is the literal meaning known? If not, it is mutashabih al-ma'na. If yes, but the referent to Allah is unclear, it is mutashabih al-murad.
5
Note the theological implication
For mutashabih al-murad, the Hanafi school typically adopts tafwid (consigning the true meaning to Allah) or ta'wil (figurative interpretation) to avoid anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پھر متشابہ دو قسم پر ہے : (۱) متشابہ المعنی: پہلی قسم وہ ہے جس کا معنی ہی معلوم نہ ہو جیسے حروف مقطعات ۔ (۲) متشابه المراد: دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ جس کا معنی معلوم ہو لیکن اس سے اللہ کی کیا مراد ہے وہ معلوم نہ ہو جیسے ید اللہ وجہ اللہ وغیرہ۔
Then the mutashabih is of two types: (1) Mutashabih al-ma'na: the first type is that whose meaning is not known at all, like the disjointed letters. (2) Mutashabih al-murad: the second type is that whose meaning is known, but what Allah intends by it is not known, like 'Yad Allah' and 'Wajh Allah' etc.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage provides a two-fold division of mutashabihat. The first category includes words whose literal meaning is unknown, such as the huruf muqatta'at at the beginning of some Quranic surahs. The second category includes words whose lexical meaning is understood (e.g., 'hand', 'face') but the intended meaning regarding Allah is not known, as Allah is not a body. This classification helps in understanding the range of ambiguity in revelation.

Key Terms
متشابہ (mutashabih): Ambiguous Quranic verses that are not muhkam (clear).
متشابہ المعنی (mutashabih al-ma'na): Type whose meaning is completely unknown.
متشابه المراد (mutashabih al-murad): Type whose meaning is known but divine intent unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Yad Allah'
B) 'Huruf muqatta'at'
C) 'Wajh Allah'
D) Clear verses like 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad'

Key Takeaway
Mutashabihat are not all alike; they divide into those with unknown meaning (mutashabih al-ma'na) and those with known meaning but unknown divine intent (mutashabih al-murad).

The Impossibility of Combining Haqiqah and Majaz
Proves Hanafi position that a word cannot simultaneously convey literal and figurative meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on combining haqiqah and majaz and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the garment analogy.

Core Idea
A single word cannot simultaneously convey literal and figurative meanings – like a garment cannot be owned and borrowed at the same time.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. While both agree that a word can potentially have both literal and figurative meanings, they differ on whether both can be intended simultaneously in one utterance. The author presents the Hanafi proof using a rational analogy, arguing that haqiqah and majaz are mutually exclusive categories, just as ownership and borrowing cannot coexist in a single garment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the area of agreement
Both Hanafis and Shafi'is agree that a word can potentially have both haqiqah and majaz meanings, and that a figurative meaning that is a specific instance of the literal general meaning can be intended.
This reduces the dispute to a narrow point.
2
State the point of disagreement
The core dispute is whether both meanings can be intended independently (not just one as subset of the other) in a single utterance.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is twofold: rational impossibility (a thing cannot be two distinct categories at once) and customary usage (no precedent exists).
The author emphasizes rational impossibility via analogy.
4
Analyze the sensory analogy
The analogy of a garment: just as a garment cannot be both owned and borrowed by the same person at the same time, a word cannot simultaneously be haqiqah and majaz.
This is a qiyas from the sensory to the rational.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is seen in bequests: the word 'mawali' is interpreted only as direct clients (haqiqah), not including their clients (majaz), because combining would require simultaneous meanings.
This shows the real-world impact of the usul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ويستحيل اجتماعهما مرادین بلفظ واحد يستحيل أن يكون الثوب الواحد على اللابس ملكاً وعارية في زمان واحد حتى قلنا ان الوصية للموالى لا تتناول موالى الموالى واذا كان له معتق واحد يستحق النصف. جمع بین الحقیقۃ والمجاز جائز ہے یا نہیں؟ امام شافعی اور ہمارے درمیان اس بات پر تو اتفاق ہے کہ ایک لفظ ظاہری اعتبار سے حقیقت اور مجاز دونوں کا احتمال رکھ سکتا ہے اور اس بات پر بھی اتفاق ہے کہ لفظ کا کوئی ایسا معنی مجازی مراد لیا جا سکتا ہے کہ حقیقت عموم مجاز کے طریقے پر اس معنی مجازی کے افراد میں سے ایک فرد ہو۔ مگر اختلاف اس بات میں ہے کہ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ ایک لفظ کے حقیقی اور مجازی معنی دونوں کو مستقل طور پر مراد لینا جائز ہے اور ہمارے نزدیک یہ جائز نہیں ہے۔ مثلا یہ نہیں ہو سکتا کہ لفظ اسد بولا جائے اور اس سے بیک وقت درندہ بھی مراد ہو اور رجل شجاع بھی۔ احناف کا استدلال ہمارے نزدیک یا تو یہ عقلاً محال ہے یا اس لئے محال ہے کہ عرف اور استعمال میں ایسا نہیں ہوتا۔ امر معقول کی امر محسوس سے مثال : مصنف نے اس کی محسوس مثال یہ دی ہے کہ لفظ بمنزلہ لباس کے ہے اور معنی بمنزلہ لباس کے ہے اور حقیقت اور مجاز بالترتیب بمنزلہ ملک اور عاریت کے ہیں جیسے یہ نہیں ہو سکتا کہ ایک لباس کا استعمال ایک ہی وقت میں ایک شخص کیلئے ملک کے طور پر بھی ہو اور عاریت کے طور پر بھی ہو اسی طرح یہ بھی نہیں ہو سکتا کہ ایک لفظ ایک ہی وقت میں حقیقت کے طور پر بھی مستعمل ہو اور مجاز کے طور پر بھی مستعمل ہو ۔ اعتراض .... جب راہن مرتضن سے ثوب مرہون کو مستعار لے کر پہنتا ہے تو اس پر یہ بات
His saying: 'And their combination is impossible as two intentions with one word' — it is impossible for a single garment to be simultaneously owned and borrowed by the wearer, so that we said: bequest to mawali does not include mawali al-mawali, and if he has one freed slave, he deserves half. Is combining haqiqah and majaz permissible or not? Between Imam Shafi'i and us there is agreement that a word can, in terms of appearance, have the potential for both haqiqah and majaz, and also that a word can be intended in a figurative meaning such that the literal general meaning (according to the way of majaz) includes one individual of that figurative meaning. But the disagreement is this: Imam Shafi'i says that it is permissible to intend both the literal and figurative meanings of one word independently, whereas in our view this is not permissible. For example, it is not possible that the word 'asad' be uttered and simultaneously mean both the beast of prey and a brave man. The proof of the Hanafis, in our view, is either that it is rationally impossible or that custom and usage do not allow it. An example of the rational from the sensory: the author gives a sensory example: that the word is like a garment, and the meaning is like the garment [؟], and haqiqah and majaz are like ownership and borrowing respectively. Just as it is impossible for a single garment to be used by one person simultaneously as owned and borrowed, similarly it is impossible for a single word to be used simultaneously as haqiqah and majaz. Objection: ... when the mortgagor borrows the mortgaged garment from the mortgagee and wears it, then on this point...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage addresses the usul dispute on whether a single word can convey both its literal (haqiqah) and figurative (majaz) meanings at the same time. The Hanafi position is that it is impossible, while Shafi'i permits it. Both schools agree that a word can potentially have multiple meanings and that a figurative meaning that is a specific instance of the general literal meaning can be intended. However, the disagreement is over independent simultaneous intention. The Hanafi proof is twofold: rational impossibility (a single thing cannot be two different categories simultaneously, like ownership and borrowing) and customary usage (people do not use words that way). The author gives a sensory analogy: a garment cannot be both owned and borrowed by the same person at the same time; similarly, a word cannot be both haqiqah and majaz in one utterance. An objection is raised regarding a case where a mortgagor borrows the mortgaged garment from the mortgagee—this is mentioned but the argument cuts off. The implication of this usul principle affects various fiqh rulings, such as the interpretation of bequests (the example of wasiyyah li'l-mawali not including mawali al-mawali).

Key Terms
حقیقت (haqiqah): The literal, original meaning of a word as established by language.
مجاز (majaz): The figurative meaning of a word, used in a sense other than its original due to a connection.
جمع بین الحقیقۃ والمجاز (jam' bayn al-haqiqah wa al-majaz): Using a single word with the intention of simultaneously conveying its literal and figurative meanings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on intending both the literal and figurative meanings of a single word at the same time?
A) Permissible and common
B) Permissible only in Quran
C) Not permissible due to mutual exclusivity
D) Permissible if context supports both

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a word cannot simultaneously convey both haqiqah and majaz, analogous to ownership and borrowing being mutually exclusive in a single garment.

The Direction of Taba'iyyah in Hanafi Usul
Explains direction of taba'iyyah via objection-answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of taba'iyyah to determine whether a relative is included in a ruling concerning another relative, distinguishing between descendants and ascendants.

Core Idea
Taba'iyyah flows downward: descendants follow ascendants, but ascendants never follow descendants.

The Hanafi school uses the principle of taba'iyyah (followership) to extend rulings to related persons without explicit mention. In the context of aman (safe conduct), a key question arises: if granting aman to a son includes his son (grandson), does granting aman to a father include his father (grandfather)? The Hanafi answer reveals a crucial directional constraint in this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling under discussion
Note the two scenarios: aman for sons includes grandsons, but aman for parents does NOT include grandparents.
This is the starting point of the objection.
2
State the objection
The objector argues by analogy: if aman for son includes grandson, then aman for parent should include grandparent. Recognize the analogical reasoning (qiyas).
The objector assumes symmetry.
3
Highlight the Hanafi principle of taba'iyyah
The response invokes taba'iyyah: inclusion of related meanings is by way of followership. Understand that taba'iyyah is unidirectional: only a follower can be attached to a principal, not vice versa.
This is the core usul rule.
4
Apply the principle to the two cases
A grandson is a follower (far') of his father, so he is included. A grandparent is an ascendant (asl) of the parent, so cannot be a follower; hence, not included.
The direction matters: followers are always descendants.
5
Derive the general rule
Taba'iyyah operates only from ascendant to descendant. When a ruling applies to a principal, it extends to his descendants, but not to his ascendants.
This rule applies beyond aman to other areas of fiqh (e.g., wudu, custody).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بخلاف الاستيمان على الاباء والامهات حيث لا يدخل الاجداد والجدات لأن ذا بطريق التبعية فيليق بالفروع دون الاصول .
دوسرا اعتراض ... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اگر ایسا ہے تو پھر جب آباء اور امھات کیلئے امان مانگا جائے تو اس میں اجداد اور جدات بھی داخل ہونے چاہئیں حالانکہ ایسا نہیں ہوتا ؟ جواب ....... لفظ کا مجاز کو شامل ہونا تابعیت کے طریقے سے ہے لہذا فروع کو تابع بنانا تو جائز ہے لیکن اصول کو تابع بنانا جائز نہیں تو ابن الابن تو فروع ہیں لیکن اجداد اور جدات تو اصول ہیں۔
He said: Contrary to granting aman for fathers and mothers, where grandparents do not enter, because this is by way of following (taba'iyyah), which is befitting for descendants but not for ascendants. Second objection: It is objected that if this is the case, then when aman is sought for fathers and mothers, grandparents should also be included, but this does not happen. Answer: The inclusion of the metaphorical meaning is by way of taba'iyyah. Therefore, making the descendant a follower is permissible, but making the ascendant a follower is not permissible. So the grandson is a descendant, but grandparents are ascendants.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage addresses a second objection in the same context of aman. The objector argues that if aman for sons includes grandsons, then aman for parents should include grandparents. The Hanafi response explains the principle of taba'iyyah (following/subordination). When a word is used metaphorically to include related meanings, it operates in the direction of subordination: the subordinate (follower) can be attached to the principal, but the principal cannot be attached to the subordinate. Since grandchildren are followers of their grandparents, they can be included; but grandparents are not followers of their grandchildren, so they are not included.

Key Terms
تابعية (taba'iyyah): The principle of following or subordination where a legal ruling extends to dependents.
اصل (asl): Principal or root; opposite of far'.
فرع (far'): Branch or derivative; a descendant or subordinate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why are grandparents not included when aman is sought for parents?
A) Because grandparents are not mentioned in the request.
B) Because taba'iyyah applies only to descendants, not ascendants.
C) Because the word 'father' cannot ever mean 'grandfather'.
D) Because grandparents are considered separate entities in aman law.

Key Takeaway
Taba'iyyah is a unidirectional principle: it extends rulings only from ascendants to descendants, never in reverse.
