Resolving Oath Contradictions with 'Umum al-Majaz
Defending 'umum al-majaz against objection of combining literal and metaphorical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of 'umum al-majaz to resolve apparent contradictions in oaths and identify the intended general metaphorical meaning.

Core Idea
In oaths, a single general metaphorical meaning can encompass both literal and figurative instances — no need to combine distinct meanings.

Consider someone who swears, 'I will not place my foot in the house of so-and-so.' The Hanafis rule that this oath is broken whether the person enters barefoot or shod, and whether the house is owned or rented. Critics object that this forces combining literal and metaphorical meanings. But the Hanafi school responds with 'umum al-majaz — a general metaphorical meaning that includes the literal as one of its instances. In this lesson, you will learn how this principle operates and why the objection misunderstands it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that the Hanafi ruling forces a combination of literal and metaphorical meanings. For 'placing foot', literal = barefoot, metaphorical = shod. For 'house', literal = owned, metaphorical = rented/loaned. The objector says applying the oath to both combines distinct meanings.
This objection arises from a misunderstanding of how metaphorical meanings can be general.
2
Understand the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafis rule that the oath is broken in all four cases: entering barefoot or shod, and whether the house is owned, rented, or loaned. The common element is 'entry' – a general concept that covers all scenarios.
The ruling is not arbitrary; it is based on the intended meaning of the oath.
3
Examine the response: 'Umum al-Majaz
The Hanafi response invokes 'umum al-majaz (generality of the metaphorical meaning). A single metaphorical expression is chosen that is broad enough to include both the literal and specific metaphorical instances as subsets. Here, 'placing foot' is a kinayah (allusion) for 'entry', which is a general metaphorical meaning covering all modes of entering.
'Umum al-majaz does not combine separate meanings; it posits one general metaphorical meaning that subsumes the literal.
4
Apply the principle
Apply the same logic to other oath scenarios. For example, if someone swears 'I will not taste food', the general metaphorical meaning 'consume' covers eating, drinking, and even tasting with the tongue, including both literal and metaphorical uses.
The condition is that the literal meaning must be one of the individuals under the general metaphorical meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وانما يقع على الملك والاجارة والدخول حافياً أو متنعلاً فيما اذا حلف لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان باعتبار عموم المجاز وهو الدخول ونسبة السكني
تیسرا اعتراض .. اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی لا اضع قدمي في دار فلان ، میں فلاں کے گھر میں قدم نہیں رکھوں گا حقیقتاً وضع قدم تو یہ ہے کہ اس کے پاؤں ننگے ہوں اور مجازا یہ ہے کہ پاؤں میں جوتے ہوں۔ حالانکہ تم یہ کہتے ہو کہ دونوں صورتوں میں حانث ہو جائیگا اس طرح دار فلاں میں حقیقت تو یہ ہے کہ مکان ملکیت کا ہو اور مجاز یہ ہے کہ اجارے اور عاریت کے طور پر ہو ۔ حالانکہ تم یہ کہتے ہو کہ ہر مکان میں داخل ہونے سے حانث ہو جائیگا تو حقیقت اور مجاز کو جمع کرنا لازم آیا ؟ جواب ..... جواب یہ ہے کہ یہ عموم مجاز کے طریقے پر ہے یعنی ایسا معنی مجازی مراد لیا گیا ہے کہ حقیقی معنی اس کا ایک فرد ہو مثال ( وضع قدم) دخول سے کنایہ ہے اور دخول ہر قسم کے دخول کو شامل
He said: And it applies to ownership, lease, and entry, whether barefoot or shod, when one swears 'I will not place my foot in the house of so-and-so', considering the general metaphorical meaning, which is entry and the relation of dwelling. Third objection: It is objected that if someone takes an oath saying 'I will not place my foot in the house of so-and-so' — literally, 'placing the foot' means his feet are bare, and metaphorically it means his feet are shod. Yet you say that in both cases he becomes a sinner (hanith). Similarly, regarding 'the house of so-and-so', literally it means the house owned, and metaphorically it means as lease or 'ariyah (loan). Yet you say that he becomes a sinner by entering any house. So it necessitates combining the literal and metaphorical? Answer: The answer is that this is by way of 'umum al-majaz (generality of the metaphorical meaning), i.e., a metaphorical meaning is intended such that the literal meaning is one of its individuals. For example, 'placing the foot' is a kinayah for entry, and entry includes all types of entry.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage deals with a third objection concerning oaths. The objection attempts to show that the Hanafi ruling forces a combination of literal and metaphorical meanings in the case of an oath about placing a foot in a house. The Hanafi response invokes the principle of 'umum al-majaz (general metaphorical meaning). According to this, a metaphorical expression can be so broad that it encompasses both the literal and the specific metaphorical instances as subsets. For example, 'placing the foot' is a kinayah (allusion) for 'entry', which includes entering barefoot or shod, owning or renting. Thus, no actual combination of distinct meanings occurs; rather, a single general metaphorical meaning is applied, covering all cases.

Key Terms
عموم المجاز ('umum al-majaz): A general metaphorical meaning that includes its specific literal and metaphorical applications.
کنایہ (kinayah): An indirect expression that alludes to something else.
حانث (hanith): One who breaks his oath and becomes sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They admit the combination is necessary.
B) They argue that only the literal meaning is intended.
C) They invoke 'umum al-majaz where a general metaphorical meaning covers all cases.
D) They say the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
'Umum al-majaz allows a single metaphorical meaning to include the literal meaning, resolving apparent contradictions in oaths.

Two Types of Connection in Isti'arah: Formal and Conceptual
Defines two types of connection for isti'arah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between formal (ittisal suri) and conceptual (ittisal ma'nawi) connections in isti'arah and apply them to examples from language and shari'ah.

Core Idea
Isti'arah requires a connection: either formal (causal) or conceptual (shared attribute).

In Hanafi usul, isti'arah (metaphorical usage) is defined by a connection (ittisal) between two things – the real meaning and the borrowed meaning. This connection can be of two types: formal (suri), where the metaphorical meaning is linked by causality or condition, and conceptual (ma'nawi), where both share a common attribute. Understanding these types is crucial for interpreting figurative language in the Qur'an and legal texts. This lesson unpacks the definitions and examples provided in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of isti'arah in Hanafi usul
Note that isti'arah is equated with all majaz; its cause is a connection between two things.
Unlike rhetoric, usul does not require tashbih.
2
Differentiate formal vs. conceptual connection
Formal connection links forms (e.g., cause-effect); conceptual connection links shared meanings (e.g., bravery).
Both are found in language and shari'ah.
3
Apply formal connection to examples
Example: rain called 'sama' because it comes from the sky (formal/causal).
Also in shari'ah: ownership via purchase.
4
Apply conceptual connection to examples
Example: brave person called 'lion' due to shared bravery.
Also in shari'ah: kafalah and hawalah both secure debt.
5
Recognize the two types in fiqh contexts
Identify whether a given metaphorical usage in a legal text is formal or conceptual.
This aids in correct interpretation of legislative analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وطريق الاستعارة الاتصال بين الشيئين صورة أو معنى كما في تسمية الشجاع اسد او المطر سماء وفى الشرعيات الاتصال من حيث السببية والتعليل نظيرا لصورت والاتصال في معنى المشروع كيف شرع نظير المعنى . علاقہ ھائے مجاز یہاں سے مصنف مجاز کے علاقات کو بیان کرتے ہیں فرمایا کہ استعارہ کا سبب یہ ہے کہ دو چیزوں کے درمیان صورتا و معنا اتصال ہو۔ اصول فقہ والوں کے عرف میں مجاز اور استعارہ دونوں ایک چیز ہے، جب کہ اہل بیان کے نزدیک استعارہ مجاز کی ایک قسم ہے اگر مجاز میں پچیس علاقوں میں سے کوئی علاقہ تشبیہ ہو تو اسے استعارہ کہا جاتا ہے اور اگر کوئی علاقہ تشبیہ نہ ہو تو اسے مجاز مرسل کہتے ہیں۔ نکہ مصنف نے مجاز مرسل کو تعبیر کیا ہے صورتا کے لفظ سے اور استعارہ کو معناً سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔ اتصال صوری کی مراد ... اتصال صوری کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ معنی مجازی کی صورت متصل ہو معنی حقیقی کی صورت کے ساتھ مثلا یہ اس کے لیے سبب ہو یا علت ہو یا شرط ہو وغیرہ مثلاً بارش کو سماء کہا جاتا ہے اس لیے کہ بارش کی صورت متصل ہے سماء کی صورت کے ساتھ یعنی بادل سے اور عرف میں کہا جاتا ہے کل ما علاك وافلك فهو سماء اور بارش ظاہر ہے بادل سے ہی برستی ہے دونوں کے درمیان یوں اتصال ہو جائے گا۔ اتصال معنوی کی مراد ... اتصال معنوی کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دونوں کسی ایک خاص معنی میں شریک ہوں جیسے کہ شجاع کو اسد کہا جاتا ہے اس لیے کہ رجل شجاع اور وہ جانور ایک مشہور معنی میں شریک ہیں اور وہ شجاعت ہے پھر جیسے یہ دونوں قسمیں حسیات اور محاورات میں پائی جاتی ہے۔ اسی طریقے سے احکام شرعیہ میں بھی پائی جاتی ہے اور شرعیات میں مسمیت اور تعلیل کے اعتبار سے اتصال، اتصال صوری کی مثال ہے کیونکہ سبب مسبب کے ساتھ اور معلول علت کے ساتھ متصل ہوتا ہے جیسے ملک شری کیسا تھ متصل ہوتی ہے اور ملک متعہ ملک رقبہ کے ساتھ متصل ہوتی ہے اور معنی شرع میں اتصال یعنی جس معنی کی وجہ سے حکم مشروع ہوا یہ اتصال معنوی کی مثال ہے۔ مثلا کفالہ اور حوالہ کے درمیان اتصال اس معنی میں ہے کہ دونوں قرض کی توثیق کے لیے ہوتے ہیں اور اسی طرح صدقہ اور ہبہ کے درمیان اتصال کہ دونوں میں تملیک بغیر عوض کی ہوتی ہے۔ قوله: والأول على نوعين احد هما اتصال الحكم بالعلة كا تصال الملك بالشراء وانه يوجب الاستعارة عن الطرفين حتى اذا قال ان اشتريت عبداً فهو حر ونوى به
His saying: And the way of isti'arah is connection between two things in form or meaning, as in calling a brave person 'lion' or rain 'sky'. In shari'ah matters, connection in terms of causality and ta'lil is like the example of form, and connection in the meaning of that which is legislated how it is legislated is like the example of meaning. From here the author explains the relations of majaz. He said that the cause of isti'arah is that between two things there is connection in form and meaning. In the usage of usul scholars, majaz and isti'arah are one and the same, whereas according to experts in rhetoric, isti'arah is a type of majaz: if any of the twenty-five relations includes tashbih, it is called isti'arah, and if no relation of tashbih, it is called majaz mursal. Note: The author expressed majaz mursal with the word 'form' and isti'arah with the word 'meaning'. The meaning of formal connection: Formal connection means that the form of the metaphorical meaning is connected to the form of the real meaning, for example it is a cause, or an illah, or a condition, etc. For example, rain is called 'sama' because the form of rain is connected to the form of sky, i.e., clouds, and in common usage, everything that is above you and the firmament is called 'sama', and rain obviously comes from clouds, thus there is connection between them. The meaning of conceptual connection: Conceptual connection means that both share a specific meaning, such as calling a brave person 'asad' because a brave man and that animal share a well-known meaning, namely bravery. Then these two types are found in sensory and figurative matters. Similarly, they are found in shari'ah rulings. In shari'ah matters, connection in terms of musammat and ta'lil is an example of formal connection because the cause is connected to the effect and the effect is connected to the cause, like ownership of shira' is connected to ownership of raqbah? And connection in meaning in shari'ah, i.e., the meaning due to which a ruling is legislated, is an example of conceptual connection. For example, connection between kafalah and hawalah in the sense that both are for securing debt, and similarly between sadaqah and hibah in that both involve transfer of ownership without compensation. His saying: And the first is of two types: one is connection of the ruling with the cause, like the connection of ownership with purchase, and it necessitates isti'arah on both sides, so that if he says 'If I buy a slave, he is free' and intends by it...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of isti'arah (metaphorical usage) in usul al-fiqh. The author states that isti'arah arises from a connection between two things, either in form (surah) or in meaning (ma'na). In usul al-fiqh, majaz and isti'arah are considered synonymous, unlike in rhetoric where isti'arah is a subset of majaz requiring a tashbih (likeness) relation. The author further explains that formal connection involves causality, condition, or other relations where the metaphorical meaning's form is linked to the real meaning's form (e.g., rain called 'sama' because it comes from the sky). Conceptual connection involves sharing a common attribute (e.g., bravery in calling a man 'lion'). These connections apply both in sensory and linguistic contexts, and also in shari'ah rulings. In shari'ah, formal connection occurs between cause and effect (e.g., ownership through purchase), and conceptual connection occurs when two legal institutions share a common purpose (e.g., kafalah and hawalah both securing debt, sadaqah and hibah both gratuitous transfer). The passage ends by introducing two subtypes of the first category, starting with the connection of a ruling to its cause.

Key Terms
استعارہ (isti'arah): Metaphorical usage where one thing is used for another due to a connection involving tashbih (likeness) in the terminology of rhetoricians, but in usul al-fiqh it is synonymous with majaz.
اتصال صوری (ittisal suri): Formal connection where the metaphorical meaning's 'form' (e.g., cause, condition) is linked to the real meaning's form.
اتصال معنوی (ittisal ma'nawi): Conceptual connection where both share a common attribute or meaning (e.g., bravery in 'lion').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of formal connection (ittisal suri) in shari'ah?
A) The connection between kafalah and hawalah because both secure debt
B) The connection between sadaqah and hibah because both are gratuitous
C) The connection between ownership and purchase because purchase causes ownership
D) The connection between bravery and lion because both share the attribute

Key Takeaway
Isti'arah in Hanafi usul is defined by a connection (ittisal) between real and metaphorical meanings, which can be formal or conceptual, and this approach unifies all figurative language under a single analytical framework.

Types of Majaz: Isti'ara and Majaz Mursal
Defining isti'ara and majaz mursal via tashbih and ittisal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between isti'ara and majaz mursal by identifying the presence or absence of tashbih, and classify examples of figurative language in legal texts according to the Hanafi usul framework.

Core Idea
Isti'ara relies on similarity (tashbih); majaz mursal on formal connection (ittisal suri) — the key distinction in Hanafi usul.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding how words are used figuratively (majaz) is essential for interpreting legal texts correctly. This passage from a classical usul text draws a clear line between two major types: isti'ara (metaphor) and majaz mursal (metonymy). The distinction rests on whether the figurative meaning is linked to the literal by similarity (tashbih) or by a formal connection such as cause-effect. Let's walk through the definitions and their application to shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types and their criterion
The passage opens by stating that when the relation (al-'alaqa) between literal and figurative meaning involves tashbih (likeness), it is isti'ara; otherwise it is majaz mursal. Note that the author uses 'suran' for majaz mursal and 'ma'nan' for isti'ara, highlighting the formal vs. meaning-based connection.
Remember: the presence of tashbih is the deciding factor.
2
Distinguish ittisal suri and ittisal ma'nawi
Ittisal suri (formal connection) is when the figurative meaning connects to the literal through external relationships like cause, condition, or effect. Ittisal ma'nawi (meaning connection) is when both share a common attribute. Examples: rain called sama' (suri) and brave man called asad (ma'nawi).
The author emphasizes that these apply to both physical and idiomatic expressions.
3
Apply to shari'ah examples
The passage extends the framework to legal rulings: purchase-ownership (cause-effect) is ittisal suri; the connection between kafala and hawala (both securing debt) is ittisal ma'nawi. Similarly, sadaqa and hiba share the attribute of transfer without compensation.
This demonstrates how figurative connections underlie legal analogies.
4
Differentiate between the two types of connection in practice
To classify a figurative usage, first test for tashbih (similarity of attribute). If present, it is isti'ara with ittisal ma'nawi. If not, check for a formal relationship (cause, effect, condition, etc.) to confirm majaz mursal with ittisal suri.
Always start with tashbih — it is the primary distinguisher.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علاقوں میں سے کوئی علاقہ تشبیہ ہو تو اسے استعارہ کہا جاتا ہے اور اگر کوئی علاقہ تشبیہ نہ ہو تو اسے مجاز مرسل کہتے ہیں۔ نکہ مصنف نے مجاز مرسل کو تعبیر کیا ہے صورتا کے لفظ سے اور استعارہ کو معناً سے تعبیر کیا ہے۔ اتصال صوری کی مراد ... اتصال صوری کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ معنی مجازی کی صورت متصل ہو معنی حقیقی کی صورت کے ساتھ مثلا یہ اسکے لئے سبب ہو یا علت ہو یا شرط ہو وغیرہ مثلاً بارش کو سماء کہا جا تا ہے اس لئے کہ بارش کی صورت متصل ہے سماء کی صورت کے ساتھ یعنی بادل کیسا تھ اور عرف میں کہا جات ہے كل ما علاك وافلك فهو سما ء اور بارش ظاہر ہے بادل سے ہی برستی ہے دونوں کے درمیان یوں اتصال یا یا جائے گا۔ اتصال معنوی کی مراد ... اتصال معنوی کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دونوں کسی ایک خاص معنی میں شریک ہوں جیسے کہ شجاع کو اسد کہا جاتا ہے اس لئے کہ رجل شجاع اور وہ جانور ایک مشہور معنی میں شریک ہیں اور وہ شجاعت ہے پھر جیسے یہ دونوں قسمیں حسیات اور محاورات میں پائی جاتی ہے ۔ اسی طریقے سے احکام شرعیہ میں بھی پائی جاتی ہے اور شرعیات میں مسمیت اور تعلیل کے اعتبار سے اتصال، اتصال صوری کی مثال ہے کیونکہ سبب مسبب کے ساتھ اور معلول علت کیساتھ متصل ہوتا ہے جیسے ملک شری کیسا تھ متصل ہوتی ہے اور ملک متعہ ملک رقبہ کیساتھ متصل ہوتی ہے اور معنی شرع میں اتصال یعنی جس معنی کی وجہ سے حکم مشروع ہوا یہ اتصال معنوی کی مثال ہے۔ مثلا کفالہ اور حو الہ کے درمیان اتصال اس معنی میں ہے کہ دونوں قرض کی توثیق کے لئے ہوتے ہیں اور اسی طرح صدقہ اور ہبہ کے درمیان اتصال کہ دونوں میں تملیک بغیر عوض کی ہوتی ہے۔
When among relations there is a relation of tashbih, it is called isti'ara; and when there is no relation of tashbih, it is called majaz mursal. The author has expressed majaz mursal with the word form (suran) and isti'ara with meaning (ma'nan). Intended meaning of ittisal suri: connection of form means that the form of the metaphorical meaning is connected with the form of the real meaning, such as it is a cause or an 'illa or a condition, etc. For example, rain is called sama' because the form of rain is connected to the form of sama', i.e., the cloud, and in custom it is said: 'Everything that is above you and the sky is sama''. And rain obviously falls from clouds, so between them there is this connection. Intended meaning of ittisal ma'nawi: meaning connection means that both share a particular meaning, such as a brave man is called 'asad (lion) because a brave man and that animal share a well-known meaning, which is bravery. Then these two types are found in physical things (hissiyyat) and in idioms. In the same way they are found in legal rulings. In legal matters, the connection in terms of name (musammat) and cause ('illa) is an example of ittisal suri; because the cause is connected to the effect and the effect to the cause, such as ownership is connected to purchase, and ownership of enjoyment (milk al-mut'a) is connected to ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqaba). And in legal meaning, ittisal refers to the meaning due to which a ruling is legislated—that is an example of ittisal ma'nawi. For example, the connection between kafala and hawala is in the sense that both are for the confirmation of debt. Similarly, the connection between sadaqa and hiba is that both involve transfer of ownership without compensation.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage defines two key types of majaz (figurative language) in usul al-fiqh: isti'ara (metaphor) and majaz mursal (metonymy). The author clarifies that the distinction rests on whether there is a tashbih (likeness) relation: if the relation between the literal and figurative meaning involves similarity (tashbih), it is isti'ara; otherwise, it is majaz mursal. He further explains that majaz mursal relies on ittisal suri (formal connection) where the figurative meaning is connected to the literal meaning through cause, effect, condition, etc. Examples include calling rain 'sama' (sky) because rain comes from clouds. Isti'ara relies on ittisal ma'nawi (meaning-based connection) where both share a common attribute, such as calling a brave man 'asad' (lion) due to bravery. The passage then applies these concepts to shari'ah, giving examples from legal categories: ownership (milk) connected to purchase (shira') via ittisal suri, and contracts like kafala and hawala both serving debt confirmation via ittisal ma'nawi. This establishes the framework for understanding how figurative language operates in legal texts and oaths.

Key Terms
استعارہ (isti'ara): A type of majaz where the connection between literal and figurative meaning involves tashbih (likeness/similarity).
مجاز مرسل (majaz mursal): A type of majaz where the connection between literal and figurative meaning does not involve tashbih but rather a formal connection such as cause, effect, condition, etc.
اتصال صوری (ittisal suri): A formal connection between the metaphorical meaning and the real meaning, such as cause-effect or condition-conditioned.
اتصال معنوی (ittisal ma'nawi): A meaning-based connection where both meanings share a common attribute (like bravery).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what distinguishes isti'ara from majaz mursal?
A) Isti'ara is used in physical things, majaz mursal in legal rulings.
B) Isti'ara involves a tashbih (likeness) relation, majaz mursal does not.
C) Isti'ara uses ittisal suri, majaz mursal uses ittisal ma'nawi.
D) Isti'ara is specific to Arabic poetry, majaz mursal to legal texts.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, isti'ara requires a tashbih relation and ittisal ma'nawi, while majaz mursal uses formal connections (ittisal suri) without tashbih.

Isti'ara bi al-Tarafayn in Oaths: Purchase and Ownership
Applying the principle of isti'ara to conditional oaths in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of isti'ara 'an al-tarafayn to derive the Hanafi ruling on conditional oaths involving purchase and ownership, and distinguish between dinan and qada'an.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, cause and effect can stand for each other metaphorically, but the dinan/qada'an distinction limits judicial acceptance.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi usul principle: isti'ara 'an al-tarafayn (metaphorical usage from both sides). By understanding that a cause can represent its effect and vice versa, we derive nuanced rulings on oaths. The source passage shows how this principle applies to the words 'purchase' (shira') and 'ownership' (milk) in oath forms, and explains when the oath-taker's intention is accepted religiously versus judicially.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause-effect pair
Recognize that purchase is the cause and ownership is the effect, as they are necessarily connected.
The passage uses 'like the connection of ownership with purchase'.
2
Understand isti'ara principle
Isti'ara 'an al-tarafayn allows mentioning the cause to mean the effect, or the effect to mean the cause, due to interdependence.
The principle: mention the independent thing (muhtaj ilayh) to refer to the dependent (muhtaj).
3
Apply to shira'→milk case
If someone says 'If I purchase...' intending ownership, isti'ara is valid. He is believed in dinan but not qada'an because of a lighter consequence.
Buying half frees half under literal purchase, but under ownership it would not free unless full ownership.
4
Apply to milk→shira' case
If someone says 'If I own...' intending purchase, he is believed in both dinan and qada'an because this interpretation is stricter.
Ownership is broader than purchase, so interpreting it as purchase restricts the oath's trigger.
5
Analyze the objection and response
The objection that both cases involve concession is resolved by noting the author only discussed dinan, not qada'an, in the second case.
The objection fails because the author's ruling is limited to dinan, where concession is acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: والأول على نوعين احد هما اتصال الحكم بالعلة كا تصال الملك بالشراء وانه يوجب الاستعارة عن الطرفين حتى اذا قال ان اشتريت عبداً فهو حر ونوى به الملك أو قال ان ملكت عبد أفهو حر ونوى به الشراء يصدق فيهما ديانة. تقسیم پہلی قسم یعنی وہ اتصال جو سبیت اور تعلیل کے اعتبار سے ہوتا ہے۔ اس کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔ (1) پہلی قسم کی تعریف یہ ہے کہ معلول کا اتصال علت کے ساتھ ہو جیسے ملک کا شراء کیساتھ اتصال اس میں طرفین سے استعارہ جائز ہے یعنی علت کو ذکر کر کے حکم یعنی معلول اور حکم کو ذکر کر کے علت مراد لی جاسکتی ہے اس لئے کہ حکم علت کا محتاج ہے ثبوت کے اعتبار سے اور علت حکم کی محتاج ہے مشروع ہونے کے اعتبار سے اور استعارہ میں اصل یہ ہے کہ محتاج الیہ کو ذکر کیا جائے اور محتاج اس سے مراد لیا جائے۔ اس کی وضاحت کیلئے مثال یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے کہا ان اشتريت عبداً فهو حر اور نیت اس شراء سے ملک کی کی تو یہ جائز ہو گا لیکن اس صورت میں ديانة تو اس کی تصدیق کی جا سكتی ہے لیکن قضاء اس کی تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی کیونکہ اس طرح اس کے حق میں تخفیف ہوتی ہے اس لئے کہ اگر اس نے آدھا خرید لیا تو شراء کی صورت میں یہ آدھا آزاد ہو جائے گا مگر ملک کی صورت میں آزاد نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ ملک میں کل کا جمع ہونا شرط ہے البتہ شراء میں یہ شرط نہیں ہے۔ اور اگر اس نے کہا کہ ان ملكت عبداً فهو حر ( اور نیت اس ملک سے شراء کی کی تو ديانة اور قضاء اس کی تصدیق کی جائے گی۔ اعتراض اعتراض یہ ہوتا ہے کہ اس صورت میں بھی تو تخفیف ہے کیونکہ ملکیت شراء کے ذریعہ ہو سکتی ہے۔ اسی طرح مہر، وصیت اور وراثت سے بھی ہو سکتی ہے۔ جب شراء ایک سبب معین کے ساتھ خاص ہے لیکن یہ اعتراض مصنف پر نہیں ہو سکتا اس لئے کہ مصنف نے صرف دیانت کو ذکر کیا ہے قضا کو ذکر نہیں کیا۔
He said: The first type is of two kinds: one of them is the connection of the ruling (hukm) with the cause ('illa), like the connection of ownership (milk) with purchase (shira'), and it necessitates isti'ara from both sides. So if someone says 'If I purchase a slave, he is free' and intends by it ownership, or says 'If I own a slave, he is free' and intends by it purchase, he is believed in both cases in dinan (religiously). Division: The first kind is that connection which is in terms of causality and ta'lil. It has two types: (1) The first type is defined as the connection of the effect with the cause, like the connection of ownership with purchase. In this, isti'ara from both sides is permissible: that is, the cause can be mentioned meaning the ruling (i.e., the effect), and the ruling can be mentioned meaning the cause, because the ruling is dependent on the cause in terms of existence, and the cause is dependent on the ruling in terms of being legislated. And the principle in isti'ara is that the thing needed (al-muhtaj ilayh) is mentioned and the needy (al-muhtaj) is intended. For clarification, an example: if a person says 'If I purchase a slave, he is free' and intends by this purchase ownership, then it is permissible, but in this case he will be believed in dinan but not in qada'an (judicially), because there is a concession in his favor. For if he purchased half, then in the case of purchase, this half would be freed, but in the case of ownership it would not be freed, because in ownership the condition is the collection of all (full ownership), but in purchase this condition is not required. And if he says 'If I own a slave, he is free' and intends by this ownership purchase, then he will be believed both in dinan and qada'an. Objection: The objection is that in this case also there is a concession, because ownership can occur through purchase, just as through mahr, bequest, and inheritance. But purchase is specific to a particular cause. However, this objection cannot be made against the author, because the author only mentioned dinan, not qada'an.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on ittisal suri by focusing on the first type: connection of a ruling (effect) with its cause. The author explains that when two things are connected as cause and effect (e.g., 'illa and hukm), isti'ara (metaphorical usage) can be applied from both sides: the cause can stand for the effect and vice versa. The principle is that the independent thing (the cause) is mentioned and the dependent thing (the effect) is intended, or the effect is mentioned and the cause intended. This is applied to oaths involving purchase (shira') and ownership (milk). If someone swears using the word 'purchase' but intends 'ownership', he is believed in dinan (religiously, i.e., in his intention before Allah) but not in qada'an (judicially, i.e., in court). The reason is that interpreting it as 'ownership' would give him a concession: if he buys half the slave, the oath would free only half, whereas if taken literally as 'purchase', buying half triggers the freeing of half, which is a lighter consequence. On the other hand, if he says 'ownership' intending 'purchase', he is believed both in dinan and qada'an because interpreting as 'ownership' is stricter (requires full ownership for freeing). The author defends this ruling by noting that the concession only appears if we consider multiple ways of acquiring ownership, but the objection is invalid because the author only addressed dinan, where such concession is acceptable. This lesson illustrates how usul principles shape practical fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
استعارہ عن الطرفين (isti'ara 'an al-tarafayn): Metaphorical usage from both sides: mentioning the cause to mean the effect, and mentioning the effect to mean the cause.
تصدیق ديانة (tasdiq dinan): Belief in religious/moral terms, i.e., accepting a person's intention in matters between him and Allah, even if evidence contradicts it.
تصدیق قضاء (tasdiq qada'an): Belief in judicial/legal terms, i.e., accepting only what can be proven in court according to legal evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a person says 'If I purchase a slave, he is free' but intends 'ownership', how is he treated?
A) He is believed neither in dinan nor qada'an.
B) He is believed in both dinan and qada'an.
C) He is believed in dinan but not in qada'an.
D) His oath is void due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, isti'ara from both sides is permissible when cause and effect are necessarily connected, but the dinan/qada'an distinction determines whether the interpretation is accepted judicially based on the presence of concession.

Cause for Effect: The Second Type of Figurative Connection
Defining the second type of istiara: cause for effect, with example and Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the cause can be used metaphorically for the effect in Hanafi usul, identify the one-way directionality, and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Cause may stand for the effect, but not vice versa, because the effect depends on the cause.

This lesson unpacks the second type of metaphorical relationship in legal language – using the cause to refer to its effect. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly interpreting utterances that can have both literal and figurative meanings. We will examine the rule, its basis, a Quranic exception, and a key dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of metaphor
Recognize that the second type of istiara is the connection between the cause (sabab) and the effect (musabbab). The cause is the action or utterance that triggers a legal result, while the effect is the result itself.
The first type (not covered here) is the connection of similarity.
2
Understand the directionality rule
Learn the Hanafi rule: the cause can be used to refer to the effect, but the effect cannot be used to refer to the cause. This is because the effect depends on the cause for its existence, while the cause does not require the effect.
This one-way direction is the core of the rule.
3
Analyze the example
Work through the example of a man saying to his slave-wife 'You are free' (itq). This can be interpreted as 'You are divorced' (talaq) because emancipation removes ownership of the corpus (milk ar-raqaba), which in turn removes ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'a) – the same effect as divorce.
The cause (emancipation) is used for the effect (divorce).
4
Note the exception
If the effect is exclusively and specifically linked to the cause, then using the effect for the cause becomes permissible. The Quranic example 'I see myself pressing wine' (Surah Yusuf 12:36) illustrates this: wine (effect) is used for grapes (cause) because pressing grapes specifically yields wine.
This is an exception, not the general rule.
5
Examine the Shafi'i dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that talaq and itq can be used interchangeably because both are based on saraya (extension) and luzum (continuity). The Hanafi response distinguishes their purposes: talaq removes a restriction, itq establishes a right. Any objection about the scope of itq is answered by noting that being a cause in general suffices.
The practical outcome: Hanafis only allow one-way, Shafi'is allow both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والثاني اتصال المسبب بالسبب كاتصال ملك المتعة بزوال ملك الرقبة فيصح استعارة السبب للحكم دون عكسه. (۲) دوسری قسم کی تعریف یہ ہے کہ مسبب کا اتصال سبب کیسا تھ ہو اور سبب سے مراد یہ ہے کہ وہ حکم کی دو طرف میں سے ایک طرف ہوتی ہے اور اس کی طرف نہ تو وجوب کی اضافت کی جاتی ہے اور نہ وجود حکم کی اور نہ ہی اس میں علت کا معنی سمجھ میں آتا ہے جیسے کہ زوال ملک متعہ کا اتصال زوال ملک رقبہ کیسا تھ کیونکہ اگر کسی نے اپنی لونڈی کو کہا ( انت حرة ) تو اس سے ملک رقبہ زائل ہو جائیگی اور اس کے زوال سے ملک متعہ بھی زائل ہو جائیگی۔ تو یہاں پر استعارہ ایک طرف سے ہوگا یعنی سبب بول کر حکم مراد لینا جائز ہے لیکن حکم بول کر سبب مراد لینا جائز نہیں ہے گویا انت حرة سے انت طالق مراد لیا جا سکتا ہے لیکن انت طالق بول کر انت حرية مراد نہیں لے سکتا۔ کیونکہ مسبب اپنے ثبوت کیلئے سبب کا تو محتاج ہے لیکن سبب مشروع ہونے کیلئے مسبب کا محتاج نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ اعتاق صرف زوال ملک رقبہ کیلئے مشروع ہے اور زوال ملک متعہ اتفاقاً حاصل ہو جاتا ہے لہذا یہ جائز نہیں ہے کہ مسبب کو ذکر کر کے سبب مراد لیا جائے لیکن اگر وہ مسبب سبب کے ساتھ خاص ہو تو پھر ایسا بھی کر سکتے ہیں جیسے اللہ تعالی کا فرمان ہے انی ارانی اعصر خمراً کہ یہاں خمر کے ذکر سے عنب مراد ہے کیونکہ عصر شراب کا وصف نہیں بلکہ عنب کا وصف ہے لیکن خمر کے عنب کے ساتھ خاص ہو نے بناء پر مسبب بول کر سب مراد لیا گیا ہے۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ طلاق بول کر اعتاق اور اعتاق بول کر طلاق مراد لیا جا سکتا ہے اس لئے کہ ان میں سے ہر ایک کی بناء سرایت اور لزوم پر ہے لہذا اس اشتراک کی بناء پر ان میں اتصال معنوی ہو گا۔ ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ طلاق ایک قید کو اٹھانے کیلئے ہے اعتاق قوت کو ثابت کرنے کیلئے ہے لہذا دونوں میں کوئی مشابہت نہیں ہے لیکن یہ اعتراض ہو سکتا ہے کہ اعتاق تو اس ملک متعہ کو زائل کرنے کے لئے ہے جو کہ ملک یمین کے طریقے پر حاصل ہو نہ وہ جو نکاح کی وجہ سے حاصل ہو۔ لہذا کوئی اشتراک اور اتصال نہیں۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ فی الجملہ سبب ہونا ہی کافی ہے۔ اور یہاں ایسا ہی ہے۔
He said: The second is the connection of the effect with the cause, like the connection of the ownership of usufruct with the removal of ownership of the corpus. So it is permissible to use the cause metaphorically for the ruling, but not the reverse. (2) The definition of the second type is that the effect is connected to the cause in a certain way, and by cause is meant that it is one of two sides of the ruling, and neither obligation is attributed to it nor existence of the ruling, nor is the meaning of a cause understood in it. For example, the connection of the removal of ownership of usufruct with the removal of ownership of the corpus. Because if someone says to his slave-girl 'You are free', then ownership of the corpus is removed and with its removal ownership of usufruct is also removed. So here the metaphor is one-sided: it is permissible to use the cause to mean the ruling, but it is not permissible to use the ruling to mean the cause. So from 'You are free' he can mean 'You are divorced', but from 'You are divorced' he cannot mean 'You are free'. Because the effect needs the cause for its establishment, but the cause does not need the effect for its legislation. This is because emancipation is only legislated for the removal of ownership of the corpus, and the removal of usufruct happens incidentally. Therefore it is not permissible to mention the effect and intend the cause. But if the effect is specific to the cause, then such is also permissible, like the saying of Allah Almighty: 'I see myself pressing wine' (Surah Yusuf 12:36). Here wine is mentioned and grapes are intended, because pressing is not a description of wine but of grapes, but since wine is specific to grapes, the effect is used to mean the cause. Imam al-Shafi'i says that divorce can be intended by emancipation and emancipation by divorce, because each of them is based on extension and continuity, so due to this commonality there is a spiritual connection. We reply that divorce is for removing a restriction, while emancipation is for establishing a right, so there is no similarity between them. But an objection can be raised: emancipation is only for removing the ownership of usufruct that arises through ownership by right of hand (milk yamin), not that which arises through marriage. So there is no commonality or connection. The answer is that in general being a cause is sufficient, and that is the case here.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn from Nur al-Anwar, expanding on the second type of metaphorical relationship (istiara) in legal language. The author defines this type as one where the cause is used to refer to the effect, like saying 'You are free' to mean divorce, because freeing a slave-wife causes the removal of marriage rights (milk al-mut'a). However, the reverse is not allowed because the cause does not depend on the effect; emancipation exists for its own sake. The author then provides a Quranic example (Surah Yusuf 12:36) where 'wine' is used for 'grapes' due to specificity. A dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i is presented: Shafi'i argues that divorce and emancipation are interchangeable because both involve legal consequences that spread. The Hanafi response distinguishes their purposes: divorce removes a restriction (nikah), while emancipation establishes a right (freedom). An objection regarding the scope of emancipation (only for milk yamin, not nikah) is answered by saying that being a cause in general suffices.

Key Terms
استعارة السبب للحكم (isticara al-sabab li al-hukm): Using the cause (e.g., emancipation) to refer to the effect (e.g., divorce) metaphorically.
مسبب (musabbab): The effect or result that follows from a cause.
سبب (sabab): The cause that brings about a legal ruling; in this context, the utterance or action that is the occasion for the effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, which of the following is correct?
A) Saying 'You are free' can mean 'You are divorced', but saying 'You are divorced' cannot mean 'You are free'.
B) Both 'You are free' and 'You are divorced' can be used interchangeably.
C) Neither 'You are free' nor 'You are divorced' can be used metaphorically for the other.
D) Saying 'You are divorced' can mean 'You are free', but the reverse is not allowed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the cause may be used metaphorically for the effect, but not the reverse, due to the effect's dependence on the cause; this rule is crucial for interpreting legal utterances.

When to Use Majaz: Haqiqa Muta'dhira and Mahjura
Explaining the rule for shifting from literal to metaphorical meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, and apply the Hanafi rule to derive the intended legal meaning.

Core Idea
When applying the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, the metaphor governs.

In legal interpretation, we normally start with the literal meaning (haqiqa). But when that literal meaning is either impossible to apply without extraordinary hardship (muta'dhira) or has fallen out of common usage (mahjura), the Hanafi school directs us to the metaphorical meaning (majaz). This lesson unpacks that rule through a classic oath example, showing how a seemingly straightforward statement must be reinterpreted in light of practical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term in question
Locate the word or phrase in the legal text that has both a literal and a metaphorical meaning. For example, the word 'palm tree' in an oath.
The term must have a known metaphorical usage.
2
Determine the feasibility of the literal meaning
Ask: can the literal meaning be applied without extraordinary hardship? If yes, it is not muta'dhira. If the literal meaning is possible but no longer used (mahjura), proceed similarly.
This step requires knowledge of common usage and context.
3
Check commonality of the metaphorical meaning
If the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, verify that the metaphorical meaning is widely recognised among speakers. Otherwise, the text remains ambiguous.
The metaphorical meaning must be known to avoid arbitrary interpretation.
4
Apply the rule: turn to majaz
Conclude that the intended meaning is the metaphorical one. In the oath example, 'eat this palm tree' is interpreted as 'eat its fruit' because eating the tree is impossible.
The legal consequence (e.g., breaking the oath) follows the metaphorical meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واذا كانت الحقيقة متعذرة أو مهجورة صير الي المجاز كما اذا حلف لا يأكل هذه النخلة أو لا يضع قدمه في دار فلان ترک حقیقت و مجاز کے مواقع مجاز کے علاقات کو بیان کرنے کے بعد اب مصنف یہ بیان فرماتے ہیں کہ کہاں حقیقت کو اور کہاں مجاز کو چھوڑا جائے گا۔ تو فرمایا کہ اگر حقیقت متعذرہ ہو یعنی مشقت کے بغیر اس تک پہنچنا ممکن نہ ہو ۔ یا حقیقت مہجورہ ہو یعنی اس تک پہنچنا ممکن ہو مگر اس کو چھوڑ دیا گیا ہو تو حقیقت کو چھوڑ کر مجاز کی طرف رجوع کیا جائے گا۔ بیان امسئله (1) حقیقت متعذرہ کی مثال یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی کہ (لا أكل من هذه النخلة) چونکہ بغیر کھجور کے درخت کا کھانا مشکل ہے لہذا مجاز مراد ہو گا باقی اس کا پھل یا ثمر کھانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا اور اگر کوئی تکلف کر کے کھجور کے درخت کو کھالے تو وہ حانث نہیں ہو گا۔ اعتراض اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ جس چیز کی قسم اٹھائی ہے وہ عدم اکل النخلة ہے اور یہ متعذر نہیں ہے۔ متعذر تو ہے اس کا کھانا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ یمین جب افی پر داخل ہو تو وہ منع کیلئے ہوتی ہے اور جو چیز ماکول نہیں وہ یمین کی وجہ سے ممنوع بھی نہیں ہوگی بلکہ یمین سے پہلے ہی منع ہوگی۔
He said: And when the literal meaning (haqiqa) is impossible (muta'dhira) or abandoned (mahjura), then one turns to the metaphorical meaning (majaz), as when someone swears 'I will not eat this date palm' or 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house'. [Then the Urdu commentary:] The places of leaving haqiqa and majaz: After explaining the relations of majaz, the author now states where haqiqa is to be left and where majaz. He said: If the haqiqa is impossible, i.e., reaching it without hardship is not possible, or the haqiqa is abandoned, i.e., reaching it is possible but it has been left, then haqiqa is left and majaz is resorted to. Explanation of the example: (1) Example of impossible haqiqa: If someone swears 'I will not eat from this date palm', since eating the date tree itself is difficult, therefore majaz is intended, so eating its fruit or dates will cause him to break the oath, but if he consumes the tree with difficulty, he does not break the oath. Objection: The thing he swore about is not eating the date palm, and that is not impossible; rather, the impossibility is in eating it. Answer: When an oath enters upon something inedible, it is for prohibition, and that which is not edible is not prohibited by the oath; rather, it is already prohibited before the oath.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining when the metaphorical meaning (majaz) takes precedence over the literal meaning (haqiqa) in legal interpretation. The rule is: if the literal meaning is either unattainable without extraordinary hardship (muta'dhira) or has fallen out of use (mahjura), one must interpret according to the common metaphorical usage. The author gives an example: a person swears not to eat a date palm tree (la akul min hadhihi al-nakhla). Since eating the tree itself is practically impossible (one cannot consume wood), the oath is interpreted metaphorically as referring to its fruit (dates). Thus, eating dates would break the oath, but chewing the tree would not, because the literal meaning is not intended. An anticipated objection points out that the impossibility is not in the object of the oath (the tree) but in the act of eating it. The answer clarifies that the oath is on the act of eating, and since the tree is not normally eaten, it is already prohibited by the law (like eating wood), so the oath does not add prohibition; rather, it reinforces the metaphorical interpretation. This lesson helps in understanding how legal texts shift from literal to figurative meanings in practice.

Key Terms
حقيقة متعذرة (haqiqa muta'dhira): Literal meaning that is impossible to apply without extraordinary hardship.
حقيقة مهجورة (haqiqa mahjura): Literal meaning that has been abandoned in common usage, even though it is still theoretically possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person swears 'I will not eat this date palm' and then eats the trunk of the tree?
A) He breaks the oath because he ate from the palm tree.
B) He does not break the oath because the literal meaning is impossible and the metaphorical meaning (dates) was not consumed.
C) He breaks the oath only if he eats dates as well.
D) The oath is invalid because the object is inedible.

Key Takeaway
When the literal meaning is impossible or abandoned, the metaphorical meaning becomes the intended legal meaning.

Haqiqah Mahjurah: When Literal Meaning Is Abandoned
Explaining the concept of abandoned literal meaning with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define haqiqah mahjurah, distinguish its two causes (shariah and custom), and apply it to determine the operative meaning in oaths and agencies.

Core Idea
When a literal meaning is abandoned in shariah or custom, the figurative meaning governs the legal ruling.

Have you ever wondered why a simple oath can have unexpected consequences? In Hanafi usul, when a literal meaning becomes impractical or prohibited, it is set aside and the customary or figurative meaning takes over. This lesson unpacks the rule of haqiqah mahjurah, showing how shariah and custom can override the apparent literal sense. Through concrete examples like litigation agency and oaths involving children, you will learn to identify when a meaning is abandoned and which meaning legally applies.

Guided Path
1
Define haqiqah mahjurah
Identify the key term: a literal meaning that is abandoned (mahjur) in shariah or custom, so that only the figurative meaning (majaz) is legally operative. Note that abandonment does not invalidate the literal meaning, but sets it aside.
The author states: 'What is abandoned in shariah is like what is abandoned in custom.'
2
Analyze the first example: foot oath
Consider the oath 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house.' Literally, it means only placing the foot. But custom treats 'placing foot' as entering. Hence, the literal meaning is abandoned in custom, and entering is the intended figurative meaning. If he enters, the oath is broken.
Custom (urf) causes abandonment here.
3
Analyze the second example: litigation agency
An agent appointed for litigation (tawkil bil khusumah). Literally, litigation is denial (juhud), which includes both truth and falsehood. But false denial is haram in shariah. Therefore, the literal meaning is abandoned in shariah, and the figurative meaning—answering the opponent—is taken. The agency is valid for answering, not for false denial.
Shariah prohibition causes abandonment.
4
Analyze the third example: oath not to speak to a child
If someone swears 'I will not speak to this child,' the literal meaning restricts the oath to childhood. However, cutting off a child (even a minor) is prohibited by shariah (hadith: mercy to young and respect to elders). Thus, the literal meaning is abandoned, and the figurative meaning—the person (dhat) regardless of age—is taken. The oath breaks if he speaks to him after adulthood.
Hadith proof: the Prophet ﷺ condemned those who do not show mercy to children.
5
Distinguish definite vs indefinite child
If the child is referred to with an indefinite noun (e.g., 'a child'), the oath remains restricted to childhood because there is no specific person to attribute the figurative meaning to. The rule applies only when the child is definite.
The author explicitly states this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقیقت مہجورہ کی مثال یہ ہے کہ مثلا اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی لا اضع قدمی فی دار فلان تو اس سے مراد مجاز ہے یعنی مطلقا دخول ۔ صرف وضع قدم مراد نہیں ہے۔ قوله : المهجور شرع كالمهجور عادة حتى ينصرف التوكيل بالخصومة الى الجواب مطلقاً۔ تفریع اور مھجور شرعاً مھجور عادت کی طرح ہے۔ مثلاً اگر کسی نے کسی کو وکیل بالخصومت بنایا تو خصومت کی حقیقت تو انکار ہے خواہ حق پر ہو یا باطل پر ہو، حالانکہ یہ تو شرعاً حرام ہے۔ لہذا اس کا مجازی معنی مراد لیا جائیگا یعنی خصم کو جواب دینا۔ قوله واذا حلف لا يكلم هذا الصبي لم يقيد بزمان صباه نیز اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی لا اكلم هذا الصبی تو یہ بچپن کے زمانے سے خاص نہیں ہو گا اسلئے کہ بچے کے ساتھ قطع رحمی کرنا شرعا مہجور ہے کیونکہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا يا من لم يرحم صغيرنا ولم يوقر كبيرنا فليس منا۔ لہذا یہاں مجازی معنی مراد ہو المعنى لا أكلم هذا الذات لہذا اگر بالغ ہونے کے بعد بھی اس سے بات کی تو یہ حانث ہو جائے گا۔ اعتراض .... اعتراض ہوتا ہے کہ آپ نے اس سے ذات مراد لی تو اس طرح تین گناہوں کو اپنے اوپر لازم کر لیا ہے: (۱) بچے پر شفقت نہ کرنا (۲) بڑے کی عزت نہ کرنا (۳) مسلمان کو تین دن سے زیادہ چھوڑنا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں قصد کا اعتبار ہے اور یہ تین گناہ التزاماً لازم ہوتے ہیں نہ کہ قصداً۔ الیکن اگر کسی نے لا اكلم صبياً نكرہ کہا تو یہ بچپن کے ساتھ ہی مقید ہوگی۔
Example of abandoned literal meaning: if someone swore 'I will not place my foot in so-and-so's house', the intended meaning is figurative, i.e., entering absolutely, not just placing the foot. [The author] said: 'What is abandoned in shariah is like what is abandoned in custom, such that the authorization for litigation turns absolutely to answering.' Tafri and mahjur in shariah are like mahjur in custom. For example, if someone appoints another as agent for litigation, the reality of litigation is denial whether it is upon truth or falsehood, even though that is prohibited in shariah. Therefore its figurative meaning is taken, i.e., answering the opponent. [The author] said: 'And if he swears not to speak to this child, it is not restricted to the time of his childhood.' Likewise, if someone swears 'I will not speak to this child', it is not specific to the period of childhood, because severing ties of kinship with a child is abandoned in shariah, as the Prophet ﷺ said: 'He who does not show mercy to our young nor respect our elders is not of us.' So here the figurative meaning is intended, meaning 'I will not speak to this person'. Therefore if he speaks to him after he becomes an adult, he breaks his oath. Objection... The objection is that by taking the meaning as the person, you have obliged three sins upon yourself: (1) not being kind to a child, (2) not respecting an elder, (3) abandoning a Muslim for more than three days. The answer is that here intent is considered, and these three sins occur incidentally, not intentionally. But if someone said 'I will not speak to a child' with an indefinite noun, it is restricted to childhood.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage states the usul rule: when a literal meaning (haqiqah) is abandoned (mahjur) in shariah or custom, the figurative meaning (majaz) is applied. The author provides two examples. First, swearing not to place a foot in a house: the literal act of placing a foot is not the intent; entering the house is the figurative meaning, as swearing to not place a foot is abandoned in custom. Second, in a litigation agency, the literal act of denying (khasaamah) is prohibited in shariah, so it is abandoned; thus the agency is interpreted as answering the opponent. Third, swearing not to speak to a child: the literal meaning is restricted to childhood, but since cutting off a child is abandoned in shariah (due to the hadith), the figurative meaning of the person is taken, so the oath is broken if he speaks to him as an adult. An objection arises that this interpretation leads to committing sins, but the answer distinguishes between intended and incidental consequences. If the child is referred to indefinitely, the oath remains restricted to childhood.

Key Terms
حقیقت مہجورہ (haqiqah mahjurah): A literal meaning that is abandoned in shariah or custom, such that only the figurative meaning is legally operative.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative or non-literal meaning that is intended when the literal meaning is abandoned.
خصومت (khusumah): Litigation; in the passage, the literal meaning is denial (juhud), which is abandoned because false denial is haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to this child', and the child becomes an adult, when does the oath break according to the rule of haqiqah mahjurah?
A) The oath breaks as soon as he speaks to the child before adulthood.
B) The oath breaks if he speaks to him after adulthood as well, because the literal meaning (restricted to childhood) is abandoned, and the figurative meaning (the person) is taken.
C) The oath never breaks because it was only about childhood.
D) The oath breaks only if he speaks to him during childhood.

Key Takeaway
The rule of haqiqah mahjurah ensures that when literal meanings lead to prohibited or impractical results, the figurative meaning that aligns with shariah or custom becomes the operative one.

Preference Between Haqiqah and Majaz: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on preference between haqiqah and majaz muta'arif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ikhtilaf on haqiqah vs. majaz muta'arif, explain the underlying principle of khalifah, and apply it to oath examples.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa prefers the literal; Sahibayn prefer the customary figurative—rooted in whether majaz replaces haqiqah in speech or in ruling.

This passage explores a foundational usul dispute within the Hanafi school: when both the literal meaning and a customary figurative meaning are in use, which takes precedence? The answer reveals a deeper disagreement about the nature of figurative language as a 'vicegerent' (khalifah) of the literal. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to derive rulings from texts where multiple meanings are plausible.

Guided Path
1
State the core ikhtilaf
Identify the two positions: Abu Hanifa holds that haqiqah is preferred (أولى) when both are in use; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that majaz muta'arif is preferred.
This is not about one being valid and the other invalid—both accept both meanings, but differ on priority.
2
Analyze the oath examples
Work through the two examples (wheat and Euphrates) and see how the ikhtilaf produces different rulings. For Abu Hanifa, only the direct, literal act breaks the oath; for the Sahibayn, customary figurative uses also break it.
The examples illustrate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the usul dispute.
3
Identify the underlying principle: khalifah
The text reveals that the ikhtilaf is built on another principle: the nature of majaz as khalifah (vicegerent) of haqiqah. Abu Hanifa views it as khalifah in speech (في التكلم) – the figurative replaces the literal in the linguistic composition. The Sahibayn view it as khalifah in ruling (في الحكم) – the figurative replaces the literal in the legal effect.
This deeper principle explains why each side reaches different conclusions.
4
Apply to the 'this is my son' example
Consider the example of saying to an older slave 'this is my son'. If meant as emancipation (figurative), both schools accept it but for different reasons. Abu Hanifa requires the literal composition to be impossible; the Sahibayn require the ruling (emancipation) to be valid but hindered.
This example clarifies the practical operation of the khalifah principle.
5
Compare and evaluate the two views
Consider the strengths of each view. Abu Hanifa's view prioritizes linguistic structure; the Sahibayn's view prioritizes customary usage and legal effect. Neither is inherently weak—it is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
The passage does not give a tarjih (preference), so both remain valid positions for fatwa depending on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله واذا كانت الحقيقة مستعملة والمجاز متعارفا فهى أولى عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله خلافا لهما كما اذا لا يأكل من هذه الحنطة أو لا يشرب من هذا الفرات۔ جب حقیقت اور مجاز متعارف ہو: اور اگر حقیقت مستعمل ہو لیکن مجاز متعارف ہو تو امام صاحب رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک حقیقت اولی ہوگی اور صاحبین کے نزدیک مجاز اولی ہو گا جیسے (۱) اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی لا اكل من هذه الحنطة تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک اس سے نفس گندم مراد ہوگی کیونکہ ابال کر اور بھون کر کھا کی جاتی ہے لیکن صاحبین کے نزدیک عموم مجاز کے طور پر گندم کا باطن مراد ہو گا لہذا روٹی کھانے سے بھی حانث ہو جائے گا۔ (۲) اسی طرح اگر کسی نے قسم اٹھائی لا اشرب من هذا الفرات تو وہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک منہ لگا کر پینے سے حانث ہوگا، اور چلو یا برتن سے پینے سے حانث نہ ہوگا۔ قوله : وهذا بناء على اصل آخر و هو أن الخليفة في التكلم عنده وعندهما في الحكم ويظهر الخلاف في قوله لعبده وهو أكبر سنا منه هذا ابني۔ بناء اختلاف : یہ اختلاف ایک دوسرے اختلاف پر مبنی ہے وہ یہ کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ تکلم میں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک مجاز حقیقت کا خلیفہ ہے حکم میں مثلا هذ ابنی جب اپنے غلام کو کہا جائے مراد اس سے حریت ہو تو یہ خلیفہ ہوگا۔ ھذا ابنی کا جس سے مراد بنوت ہے امام صاحب کے نزدیک مجاز کے صحیح ہونے کیلئے ضروری ہے کہ حقیقت اصل عربی ترکیب کے اعتبار سے صحیح ہو تو اگر معنی حقیقی نہیں بن سکا تو معنی مجازی مراد لیا جائے گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک مجاز چونکہ حقیقت کا خلیفہ ہے حکم میں لہذا اگر حکم صحیح ہو مگر کسی عارض کی وجہ سے اس پر عمل نہ کیا جا سکے تو مجاز کی طرف رجوع کیا جائے گا۔
[The author] said: 'And if the literal meaning is used and the figurative is customary, then the literal is better according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to them, as when he says: I will not eat from this wheat, or I will not drink from this Euphrates.' When literal and figurative are customary: And if the literal is used but the figurative is customary, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the literal is better, and according to the two companions the figurative is better. For example, (1) if someone swears 'I will not eat from this wheat', according to Imam Abu Hanifa the intended meaning is the wheat grain itself because it can be eaten after boiling or roasting, but according to the two companions, the figurative meaning is general: the interior of the wheat, so eating bread also breaks the oath. (2) Similarly, if someone swears 'I will not drink from this Euphrates', according to Imam Abu Hanifa he only breaks the oath by putting his mouth to it and drinking, but drinking from a ladle or vessel does not break it. [The author] said: 'And this is based on another principle: that the vicegerency in speech is with him and with them in ruling, and the difference appears in his saying to his slave, who is older than him: This is my son.' The basis of the difference: This disagreement is based on another disagreement, which is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the figurative is the vicegerent of the literal in speech, while according to the two companions, the figurative is the vicegerent of the literal in ruling. For example, when one says 'This is my son' to his slave, meaning emancipation, this is a vicegerent. Regarding 'This is my son' which means sonship, according to Imam Abu Hanifa for the figurative to be valid it is necessary that the literal original is valid in terms of Arabic composition; if the literal meaning is not possible, then the figurative meaning is taken. According to the two companions, since the figurative is the vicegerent of the literal in ruling, if the ruling is valid but due to some obstacle it cannot be acted upon, then recourse is made to the figurative.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (ikhtilaf) in Hanafi usul regarding the preference when both the literal meaning (haqiqah) and a customary figurative meaning (majaz muta'arif) are in use. The matn states that according to Abu Hanifa, the literal is preferred; according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the figurative is preferred if customary. Two examples illustrate: swearing not to eat wheat (Abu Hanifa: only the grain; Sahibayn: also bread as figurative) and swearing not to drink from the Euphrates (Abu Hanifa: only directly from the river; Sahibayn: also drinking its water from a vessel). The root of this ikhtilaf is a deeper principle: whether the figurative is the vicegerent (khalifah) of the literal in speech (Abu Hanifa) or in ruling (Sahibayn). Abu Hanifa's view means the figurative can only replace the literal when the literal is impossible in the original Arabic construction. The Sahibayn's view means the figurative replaces the literal when the ruling (the legal effect) is hindered by an impediment, even if the literal is possible in speech. The example of saying 'this is my son' to a slave clarifies: if meant as emancipation (figurative for freeing), it works as vicegerent in ruling, but if meant as literal sonship, it fails.

Key Terms
خلیفہ (khalifah): Vicegerent or substitute; in this context, the relationship of figurative meaning (majaz) to literal meaning (haqiqah) as a replacement.
مجاز متعارف (majaz muta'arif): A figurative meaning that is commonly known and conventionally used in the language.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if someone swears 'I will not drink from this Euphrates', which action breaks the oath?
A) Drinking from the river with a ladle.
B) Putting his mouth to the river and drinking directly.
C) Drinking from the river using a cup.
D) Any drinking of water from that river.

Key Takeaway
The preference between haqiqah and majaz muta'arif depends on the underlying usul principle of whether majaz is a vicegerent in speech (Abu Hanifa) or in ruling (Sahibayn), leading to different outcomes in oath and other legal contexts.

The Legal Meanings of the Particle 'Ala in Hanafi Usul
Explains the meanings of 'ala: obligation, exchange, and condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three distinct legal meanings of 'ala (obligation, exchange, and condition) and apply the correct meaning to a given legal phrase.

Core Idea
The single particle 'ala bears three distinct legal meanings depending on context—obligation, exchange, or condition—and misidentification changes the ruling.

In legal language, particles are not arbitrary; their meanings can shift based on context, affecting entire rulings. This passage from Khulasat al-Anwar explains how the Arabic particle 'على' operates in three different legal contexts: as the primary indicator of obligation, as a substitute for the exchange particle 'باء' in contracts, and as a marker of condition in divorce according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Mastering these distinctions is essential for accurate istinbat (derivation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meaning
Recognize that 'ala defaults to obligation (ilzam) as in debt statements. Look for the condition that changes it: attachment of 'wadiah' (deposit) negates the debt meaning.
The default is obligation; the exception is when 'wadiah' is attached.
2
Recognize the exchange meaning
When 'ala enters pure mu'awadat (sale, lease, marriage), it means like the particle ba', indicating exchange. The illa is that exchange is the essence in these contracts.
This is a context-driven shift, not an exception.
3
Trace the dispute in divorce
The two companions (Sahibayn) extend the exchange meaning to divorce, while Abu Hanifa treats it as a condition, citing the Quranic allegiance verse. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: under Sahibayn, a single divorce triggers payment; under Abu Hanifa, the divorce is conditional.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf recorded without explicit tarjih in this text.
4
Apply the meaning to a new scenario
Practice: Given a phrase, determine which meaning applies and state the resulting legal effect. Use the conditions (wadiah, exchange context, divorce context) to guide your decision.
Context is decisive; always look at the surrounding terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وعلى للإلزام فقوله له على ألف درهم يكون دينا إلا أن يتصل بها الوديعة. ۲- حرف على : على التزام كيلئے آتا ہے کیونکہ یہ لغت میں استعلاء كيلئے ہوتا ہے خواہ استعلاء حقیقتا ہو یا حکماً لہذا اگر کسی نے کہا ( علی ألف درهم ) تو اس سے مراد قرض ہو گا إلا یہ کہ اس کے ساتھ لفظ ودیعت متصل مذکور ہو۔ قوله : فان دخلت في المعاوضات المحضة كانت بمعنى الباء - اور اگر علی معاوضات محضہ میں داخل ہو تو یہ باء کے معنی میں ہوگا جیسے بیع اجارہ اور نکاح وغیرہ کیونکہ ان چیزوں میں معاوضہ اصل ہوتا ہے۔ قوله : وكذا اذا استعملت في الطلاق عند هما وعند أبي حنيفة للشرط . صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر ( علی ) طلاق میں استعمال ہو تو بھی (باء) کے معنی میں ہوگا کیونکہ طلاق میں جب عوض پر داخل ہو جاتا ہے تو وہ معاوضات کے معنی میں ہو جاتی ہے تو اگر عورت نے اپنے شوہر سے کہا طلقني ثلاثا علی ألف درهم اگر شوہر نے ایک طلاق دی تو اس کو ہزار کا ٹکٹ ملے گا اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ علی شرط کیلئے ہے جیسا کہ قرآن میں ہے بايعنك على أن لا يشركن بالله کیونکہ جیسے علی الزام کیلئے ہوتا ہے اسی طرح جزاء
His saying: And 'ala is for obligation, so his saying 'he has upon me a thousand dirhams' is a debt unless the word 'deposit' is attached to it. 2- The letter 'ala: 'ala comes for commitment because in language it is for superiority, whether real or ruling. So if someone says 'he has upon me a thousand dirhams', the intended meaning is a loan, unless the word 'deposit' is explicitly attached. His saying: If it enters into pure exchanges, it is in the meaning of ba'. And if 'ala enters into pure exchanges, it will be in the meaning of ba', like sale, lease, marriage, etc., because in these things exchange is the basis. His saying: And similarly when used in divorce according to them (the two companions), and according to Abu Hanifa it is for condition. The two companions say that if 'ala is used in divorce, it is also in the meaning of ba', because when it enters upon a consideration, it becomes in the meaning of exchanges. So if a woman says to her husband 'divorce me three times for a thousand dirhams', and the husband gives one divorce, she will get the money. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, this 'ala is for condition, as in the Quran 'those who swear allegiance to you upon that they will not associate anything with Allah', because just as 'ala is for obligation, similarly it is for recompense.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage explains the different meanings of the Arabic particle 'على' (ala) in legal contexts. The author states that 'ala primarily indicates obligation (ilzam), so when someone says 'علي ألف درهم' (I owe a thousand dirhams), it creates a debt unless the word 'وديعة' (deposit) is attached, which would change the meaning. The text then gives two additional rules: (1) When 'ala enters into pure exchanges (mu'awadat mahdah) like sale, lease, or marriage, it means like the particle 'باء' (for exchange/consideration). (2) Regarding divorce, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that if 'ala is used, it also means like ba', so if a wife says 'طلقني ثلاثا على ألف' (divorce me three times for a thousand), and the husband gives one divorce, she is entitled to the money. However, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that 'ala in divorce indicates a condition (shart), like the verse 'بايعنك على أن لا يشركن بالله' (they pledge allegiance to you upon that they will not associate), where 'ala is a condition. This ikhtilaf among the Hanafi authorities is recorded as a recognized difference of opinion within the madhhab.

Key Terms
على ('ala): A particle meaning obligation (ilzam) primarily, but also used for exchange (as ba') or condition.
الإلزام (al-ilzam): Obligation; the primary meaning of 'ala.
المعاوضات المحضة (al-mu'awadat al-mahdah): Pure exchanges where consideration is the essence, e.g., sale, lease, marriage.
شرط (shart): Condition; the meaning of 'ala according to Abu Hanifa in divorce contexts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'ala when used in pure exchanges like sale or marriage?
A) Obligation (ilzam).
B) Like the particle ba' (for exchange).
C) Condition (shart).
D) Extra (za'idah).

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'ala in legal texts is context-dependent: it signals obligation by default, shifts to exchange in contracts, and may indicate condition in divorce—a distinction that directly affects the resultant ruling.

The Linguistic Dispute Over 'Idha' and Its Impact on Conditional Divorce in Hanafi Usul
Analyzing the conditional particle idha and its effect on divorce oaths with Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle regarding the particle 'idha' to derive the ruling on conditional divorce and explain the divergence between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The particle 'idha' in conditional divorce triggers a dispute: is it pure condition or retains time? The ruling changes accordingly.

This passage examines the Kufi and Basran grammatical analyses of 'idha' and how Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ in deriving the divorce ruling from the husband's statement. Understanding this linguistic dispute is critical for applying usul al-fiqh to conditional oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Source Text
The husband's statement 'idha lam utalliqka fa-anti taliq' (if I do not divorce you, you are divorced) is the basis for the dispute. Note the particle 'idha' introduces a condition.
The statement is a conditional clause with 'idha'.
2
Analyze the Linguistic Nature of 'Idha'
According to Kufi grammarians, 'idha' can indicate both time and condition; when the protasis (jaza) is stated, the temporal meaning drops. According to Basrans, 'idha' is primarily for time and even when used for condition, time remains.
The Kufi view supports Imam Abu Hanifa; the Basran view supports the Sahibayn.
3
Apply the Linguistic Analysis to the Legal Ruling
Imam Abu Hanifa (Kufi view): 'idha' is pure condition, so the conditional divorce occurs only when the condition is fulfilled (i.e., the husband never divorces and approaches death). Sahibayn (Basran view): 'idha' retains time, so the moment the husband finishes speaking, there exists a time when he did not divorce, triggering immediate divorce.
The difference hinges on whether the temporal meaning is dropped or retained.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The practical difference is the timing of the divorce: immediate (Sahibayn) vs. at the approach of death (Abu Hanifa). This affects the wife's status, inheritance, and iddah.
The dispute has real-world legal consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور (اذا) نحاة کوفہ کے نزدیک وقت اور شرط دونوں کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے لہذا کبھی تو اس کی جزا لائی جاتی ہے اور کبھی نہیں لائی جاتی شرط کی مثال جیسے یہ شعر واستغن ما اغناك ربك بالغنى واذا تصبك خصاصة فتحمل جزاء کی مثال واذا تكون كريهة ادعى الها واذا يحاس الحيس يدعى جندب اور جب اس کی جزاء لائی جائے تو وقت کا معنیٰ ساقط ہو جائے گا لیکن نحاة بصرہ کے نزدیک یہ حقیقت وقت کیلئے ہے اور کبھی کبھی شرط کے لئے بھی استعمال ہوتا ہے لیکن وقت کا معنی اس سے ساقط نہیں ہوتا جیسے کہ (متی) اس کے لئے جزاء لا زم ہے اس کے باوجود اس سے وقت کا معنی ساقط نہیں پہلا قول امام اعظم امام ابو حنیفہ کا ہے اور دوسرا صاحبین کا ہے۔ ہذا اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا اذالم اطلقك فانت طالق تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک پہلے وقت کا معنی ساقط ہے لہذا اطلاق اس وقت واقع ہوگی جب ان میں سے ایک مر نے کے قریب ہو جائے ۔ اور صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ وقت کا معنی ساقط نہیں ہے لہذا شوہر جو نئی بات کر کے واپس ہو گا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی کیونکہ اس کا معنی یہ ہے کہ جس وقت میں تجھے طلاق نہ دوں تجھے طلاق ہے اور جب وہ کلام سے فارغ ہو گیا تو ایسا زمانہ پایا گیا جس میں اس نے طلاق نہیں دی لہذا اطلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اور از اما اذا کی طرح ہے مگر اس میں اختلاف نہیں۔
According to the grammarians of Kufa, (idha) can function both for time and condition. Therefore, sometimes its result (jaza) is brought and sometimes not. An example of condition: the verse 'And seek self-sufficiency while your Lord enriches you with wealth, and if affliction befalls you, then endure.' An example of result: 'When a distressing event occurs, I call upon my lord; and when dates are mixed, Jundub is called.' And when its result is brought, the meaning of time is dropped. But according to the grammarians of Basra, it is originally for time and is sometimes used for condition, but the meaning of time is not dropped from it. Just like (mata), it necessarily has a result, yet the meaning of time is not dropped from it. The first opinion is of Imam al-A'zam Abu Hanifa, and the second is of the two companions (Sahibayn). Therefore, if someone says to his wife 'idha lam utalliqka fa-anti taliq' (if I do not divorce you, you are divorced), then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the meaning of time is dropped first, so divorce occurs when one of them is near death. And the two companions say that the meaning of time is not dropped, so when the husband revokes [the statement] by saying something new, divorce occurs because the meaning is: 'at the time when I do not divorce you, you are divorced.' And when he finishes speaking, a time period is found in which he did not divorce, so divorce occurs. And 'inna' is like 'idha' but there is no disagreement in it.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage discusses the linguistic particle 'idha' from the perspective of grammarians and its application in Hanafi fiqh regarding conditional divorce. The author first presents the Kufi view that idha is used for both time and condition, hence sometimes the protasis (jaza) is explicitly stated and sometimes not. He gives poetic examples. When the protasis is stated, the temporal meaning is dropped according to Kufis. The Basran view is that idha is fundamentally for time, and it can be used for condition but retains temporal meaning. This grammatical dispute maps onto a fiqh dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the divorce statement 'idha lam utalliqka fa-anti taliq' (if I do not divorce you, you are divorced). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, because idha primarily indicates condition, the time meaning is dropped, so the condition is a future one: divorce occurs only when the husband never divorces her and approaches death. The Sahibayn interpret it as 'at the time when I do not divorce you, you are divorced' meaning as soon as he finishes speaking, there exists a time in which he did not divorce, so divorce occurs immediately. The author notes that 'inna' is similar to 'idha' but there is no dispute in that case.

Key Terms
اذا (idha): A particle that can indicate both time and condition; according to Kufi grammarians it is used for both, with temporal meaning dropped when protasis is stated; according to Basran grammarians it is primarily for time.
شرط (shart): Condition, the condition clause in a conditional sentence.
جزاء (jaza): Result or protasis, the consequent clause in a conditional sentence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon saying the statement
B) When the husband dies or is near death
C) When the wife demands it
D) It is not valid as a conditional divorce

Key Takeaway
The linguistic analysis of the particle 'idha'—whether it retains temporal meaning or not—directly determines the timing of divorce in conditional statements, illustrating the deep connection between usul al-lughah and fiqh rulings.
