The Particle 'Law' as a Future Conditional in Divorce
Explaining that 'law' is used for condition in divorce oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the particle 'law' in conditional divorce and apply it to new scenarios.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'law' in divorce oaths functions as a future conditional, not a past hypothetical.

This lesson examines a specific usul al-fiqh point: the treatment of the Arabic particle 'law' when used in divorce oaths. Although its primary meaning is for past hypotheticals, Hanafi jurists reinterpret it as indicating a future condition (like 'in') in such contexts. This ruling is based on the customary usage (urf) in divorce and the principle that the intention of the speaker is given weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its primary meaning
Note that 'law' (لو) in classical Arabic primarily introduces a hypothetical condition referring to the past. For example, 'law ja'ani Zaidun la akramtuhu' means 'If Zaid had come to me, I would have honored him.'
2
Recognize the context of divorce
When a husband uses 'law' in a divorce statement (e.g., 'Anti taliq law dakhalti al-dar'), the context shifts from past hypothetical to future condition.
The Hanafi school considers the customary usage (urf) of divorce oaths.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
Apply the rule that 'law' in such contexts is equivalent to 'in' (ان), a particle for future condition. Therefore, the divorce is conditional upon the future occurrence of the act.
This is a legal (shar'i) interpretation that overrides the literal linguistic meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولو للشرط - اور (لو) شرط کے لئے آتا ہے تو اگر کسی نے اپنی رفیقہ حیات کو کہا انت طالق لو دخلت الدار تو ایسا ہے جیسے ان دخلت الدار اس نے کہا کیونکہ اگر چہ (لو) اپنے معنی اصلی کے اعتبار سے ماضی میں استعمال ہوتا ہے لیکن یہاں (ان) کے معنی میں ہو کر مستقبل کے لئے ہوگا۔
And (law) is used for condition. So if someone says to his wife 'Anti taliq law dakhalti al-dar' (you are divorced if you enter the house), then it is as if he said 'in dakhalti al-dar' because even though (law) in its original meaning is used for past tense, here it takes the meaning of (in) and becomes for the future.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This short passage clarifies that the Arabic particle 'law' (لو) in classical Arabic primarily indicates a hypothetical past condition, but in the context of conditional divorce, it is treated as equivalent to 'in' (ان) which indicates future condition. Therefore, the statement 'anti taliq law dakhalti al-dar' is a valid conditional divorce that will take effect when the wife enters the house.

Key Terms
لو (law): A conditional particle originally for past hypotheticals, but in divorce oaths it is used for future condition like 'in'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'انت طالق لو دخلت الدار', when does the divorce occur according to the Hanafi school?
A) Immediately upon the statement
B) When the husband dies
C) When the wife enters the house
D) It is not a valid conditional divorce because 'law' is for past

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the particle 'law' in conditional divorce is treated as a future conditional equivalent to 'in', based on customary usage (urf).

The Usul Principle: 'Kayfa' as Interrogative – When Context Invalidates Meaning
Analyzing 'kayfa' as interrogative and its validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that an interrogative particle becomes meaningless when the context precludes a genuine question, and identify its effect on legal oaths.

Core Idea
If a word's literal meaning contradicts the context, it is void; 'kayfa' in unconditional manumission triggers immediate freedom.

This lesson examines the usul principle behind the word 'kayfa' when used in oaths of divorce and manumission. We will see how the Hanafi school analyzes linguistic context to determine if the interrogative retains its function or becomes void. The key is that linguistic usage governs legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal meaning of 'kayfa'
Recall that 'kayfa' is an interrogative particle used to ask about state or condition (e.g., 'kayfa haluka?'). It presumes a context in which a question about state is meaningful.
2
Determine when the context permits a genuine question
In an unconditional manumission or divorce oath, there is no actual question being asked—the speaker is making a declaration. Therefore, the literal function of 'kayfa' is unsuited to the context.
This is the core of the Hanafi analysis: the word is evaluated based on its suitability in the given situation.
3
Apply the principle: if context precludes question, 'kayfa' becomes meaningless
Imam Abu Hanifa rules that 'kayfa' is treated as invalid (batil) because it cannot serve its purpose. Consequently, the oath is enforced immediately without condition.
But in divorce, one divorce still occurs due to the nature of the act; the surplus is delegated.
4
Note the exception in divorce
Divorce is inherently divisible; thus only one divorce occurs immediately, and the rest (description and number) are delegated to the wife subject to the husband's intention.
This shows how the same principle applies differently depending on the legal nature of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكيف للسوال عن الحال في أصل وضع اللغة فان استقام فيها والا بطل والا بطل ولذلك قال ابو حنيفة رحمه الله في قوله انت حر كيف شئت انه ايقاع وفي الطلاق تقع الواحدة ويبقى الفضل فى الوصف والقدر مفوضاً اليها بشرط نية الزوج وقالا مالم يقبل الاشارة فحاله ووصفه بمنزلة أصله فيتعلق الاصل بتعلقه ابو حنيفة يقول يلزم من هذا اتباع ا لاصل للوصف وهو خلاف القياس . اور (کیف) حال کے متعلق سوال کرنے کے لئے آتا ہے جیسے كيف حالك اور اگر سوال کرنے کا معنی صحیح نہ ہو تو کیف کا لفظ باطل ہو جائے گا اس وجہ سے امام صاحب فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے اپنے غلام سے کہا انت حر کیف شئت وہ فوری طور پر آزاد ہو جائے گا اور (كيف) لغو ہو جائے گا ۔ اور انت طالق کیف شیئت کے ذریعہ طلاق دینے کی صورت میں ایک طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور وصف اور قدر میں زیادتی عورت کے حوالے ہوگی لیکن اس میں شوہر کی نیت شرط ہے وصف کا مطلب ہے بائنہ ہونا اور قدر کا مطلب ہے کہ تین یا دو طلاقوں کا ہونا اگر میاں بیوی کی نیت متفق ہو تو ان کی نیت کے مطابق طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور اگر نیت مختلف ہو تو تعارض کی وجہ سے دونوں نہیں ساقط ہو جائینگی ۔ کیونکہ مشہور قاعدہ ہے اذاتی رنسا تساقطا ۔ اور اصل طلاق
And (kayfa) is for asking about state in the original linguistic usage. If it is suitable (for questioning), then it stands; otherwise it becomes invalid. Therefore, Imam Abu Hanifa said regarding the statement 'Anta hurr kayfa shi'ta' (you are free as you wish) that it is an immediate manumission. And in divorce, one divorce occurs, and the remainder in description and quantity is delegated to her on condition of the husband's intention. The two companions said: as long as it does not accept indication, then its state and description are like its origin, so the origin is attached to its attachment. Abu Hanifa says that would necessitate following the origin in the description, which is against analogy. And (kayfa) is used for asking about state, like 'kayfa haluka' (how are you?). If the meaning of asking is not correct, then the word 'kayfa' becomes invalid. For this reason, Imam Abu Hanifa says that if someone says to his slave 'Anta hurr kayfa shi'ta', he becomes immediately free, and 'kayfa' is considered meaningless. And in the case of divorce using 'anti taliq kayfa shi'ti', one divorce occurs, and the surplus in description and quantity is delegated to the wife, but this is conditional upon the husband's intention. Description means being ba'in (irrevocable), and quantity means three or two divorces. If the intentions of husband and wife agree, divorce occurs according to their intention. If intentions differ, then due to conflict both intentions are dropped, because the established rule says 'when two matters conflict, they both drop'. And the original divorce...
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage deals with the interrogative particle 'kayfa' (كيف) when used in oaths of manumission and divorce. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that in 'anta hurr kayfa shi'ta' (you are free as you wish), the word 'kayfa' becomes meaningless because the context does not allow for an actual question about state; therefore immediate freedom occurs. In divorce, 'anti taliq kayfa shi'ti' results in one divorce taking place immediately, while additional aspects like whether it is ba'in or the number of divorces are delegated to the wife, subject to the husband's intention. The two companions (Sahibayn) have a different view: they consider that the description and quantity are attached to the original act, so the condition must be accepted. The passage ends with the rule that when intentions conflict, they both drop.

Key Terms
كيف (kayfa): Interrogative particle for asking about state; in divorce/manumission oaths it becomes meaningless if questioning is inapplicable.
وصف (wasf): The quality of the divorce (e.g., ba'in)
قدر (qadr): The quantity of divorces (e.g., two or three)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle explained in this lesson, what is the immediate effect of saying 'anta hurr kayfa shi'ta' to a slave?
A) The slave is free only when he wishes
B) Immediate freedom
C) The manumission is invalid
D) The slave becomes free after a period

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, when the context invalidates the literal meaning of an interrogative, the word is treated as nonexistent, leading to immediate enforcement of the oath's core.

Intra-Madhhab Dispute: Immediate vs Contingent Divorce with 'Kayfa Shi'ta'
Intra-madhhab dispute on 'kayfa' in divorce/manumission oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of 'kayfa shi'ta' in divorce and manumission, and identify the practical difference.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: one divorce immediate, rest delegated subject to intention; Sahibayn: whole contingent on acceptance.

This lesson dives into the specific dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students on how to treat the word 'kayfa' when used in conditional divorce and manumission. Understanding this shows how usul principles lead to different rulings, and highlights the importance of the mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
State the context: 'kayfa shi'ta' in divorce/manumission
Both schools are analyzing the same phrase, but they disagree on the legal effect due to differing interpretations of the word 'kayfa'.
2
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling and reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that 'kayfa' becomes invalid (batil) because the context does not allow a genuine question. In manumission, immediate freedom; in divorce, one divorce occurs immediately, and the surplus (ba'in, number) is delegated to the wife subject to the husband's intention.
This is based on the usul principle that words are interpreted according to their suitability in context.
3
Identify Sahibayn's ruling and reasoning
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the description and quantity are attached to the origin, so the condition (the wife's acceptance) must be given effect. Thus, no immediate divorce occurs; it is contingent on the wife's acceptance.
Their reasoning is that the state and description are like the origin.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, one divorce is effective immediately; according to Sahibayn, no divorce occurs until the wife accepts the condition as stated.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text presents Imam Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position. The dispute is recorded, but the operative ruling for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's.
This shows the hierarchy within the Hanafi school.

Source Text

Place Particles in Talaq: Haythu and Ayna
Textual analysis of place particles in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'haythu' and 'ayna' require the wife's wish to be expressed in the same session and distinguish between place and time particles in conditional talaq.

Core Idea
Place particles in talaq restrict the wife's option to the same session; time particles give her unrestricted choice.

This passage examines how the linguistic category of particles affects the ruling of conditional talaq. The Hanafi school holds that divorce is not inherently attached to a location, so when place particles are used, the wife must express her wish within the same session. In contrast, time particles allow her to wish at any future time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particles
Recognize that 'haythu' and 'ayna' are nouns of place, while 'idha' and 'mata' are particles of time. This classification is the basis for the different rulings.
2
Apply the Hanafi principle on place
Understand that divorce is not bound to any specific location. Therefore, when a husband says 'Anti taliq haythu shi'ti', the wife's wish is not automatically fulfilled by any place but must occur in the session where the utterance was made.
3
Derive the ruling for place particles
Conclude that divorce only occurs if the wife expresses her wish in the same session. If she wishes later, it does not take effect.
Contrast with 'idha' and 'mata' where the wish can be at any time.
4
Contrast with time particles
Note that 'idha' and 'mata' indicate generality of time, so the wife's wish is not restricted to the session. She may wish at any time, and divorce occurs when she does.
This distinction is rooted in the linguistic meaning of the particles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وحيث وأين اسمان للمكان فاذا قال انت طلاق حيث شئت أو أين شئت انه لا يقع ما لم تشاء وتتوقف مشيتها على المجلس بخلاف اذا ومتى . اور ( حیث) اور (این ) مکان کے اسم ہیں تو اگر کسی نے کہا انت طالق حيث شئت یا این شئت تو چونکہ طلاق کسی مکان کے ساتھ مخصوص ہوتی نہیں تو اس وقت طلاق واقع ہوگی جب وہ عورت چاہے گی لیکن اس کی مشیت مجلس پر موقوف ہوگی بخلاف اذا اور متی کے کیونکہ وہ عموم زمان پر دلالت کرتے ہیں لہذا ان میں مشیت مجلس پر موقوف نہیں ہوا کرتی ہے۔
He said: And 'haythu' and 'ayna' are nouns for place. So if he says 'You are divorced where you wish' or 'wherever you wish', it does not occur until she wishes, and her wish is restricted to the session, unlike 'idha' and 'mata' because they indicate generality of time, so in them the wish is not restricted to the session. (Urdu) And (haythu) and (ayna) are nouns for place. So if someone said 'You are divorced where you wish' or 'wherever you wish', because divorce is not specific to any place, divorce will occur when that woman wishes, but her wish is restricted to the session, contrary to 'idha' and 'mata' because they indicate generality of time, therefore in them the wish is not restricted to the session.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between particles of place (haythu, ayna) and particles of time (idha, mata) in conditional talaq. Since divorce is not inherently linked to a location, the wife's wish to be divorced must be expressed in the same session (majlis) where the husband made the statement. If she wishes in that session, divorce occurs. In contrast, when time particles are used (idha, mata), the wife's wish is not confined to the session because time particles have general applicability over time.

Key Terms
حیث (haythu): A noun for place; in talaq, requires wife's wish in the session.
أين (ayna): A noun for place; same ruling.
مجلس (majlis): The session in which the utterance is made.
اذا (idha): A particle of time indicating generality; in talaq, wife's wish not restricted to session.
متى (mata): A particle of time; same ruling as idha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The wife must wish in the same session

Key Takeaway
Place particles in talaq condition the wife's wish to the same session, whereas time particles allow indefinite choice.

Sarih: Definition and Ruling on Intention
Defining sarih and its legal effect without intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sarih, give its examples, and state the Hanafi rule that sarih expressions are effective without intention.

Core Idea
Sarih expressions are effective by their wording alone; intention is irrelevant.

In this passage, the author defines sarih (explicit expression) and explains its unique ruling in Hanafi law. Unlike kinayah (allusive expressions), sarih does not require the speaker's intent to produce a legal effect. The examples of divorce and manumission illustrate the principle: if you utter 'You are divorced' or 'You are free', the divorce or emancipation is legally effective regardless of what you intended to say. This lesson will help you understand this foundational distinction in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Read the definition of sarih: a word whose intended meaning is clear, whether literal or figurative. Note the key phrase 'manifestly clear'.
Sarih can be haqiqah (literal) or majaz (figurative), as long as the meaning is obvious.
2
Understand the ruling: legal effect attaches to wording
The text states that for sarih, the ruling is that the legal effect is attached to the very speech, not to the speaker's intention. This means intention is not necessary.
Compare with kinayah where intention is required. This is a key distinction.
3
Apply to the given example
Consider the example: someone intends to say 'Subhanallah' but says 'Anti taliq'. According to the rule, divorce occurs because 'Anti taliq' is sarih.
The ruling is based on the wording itself, not the speaker's hidden intention.
4
Note the scope of sarih
The text mentions divorce and emancipation as primary examples. In Hanafi fiqh, sarih also applies to other areas like oaths and acknowledgments.
The principle is general for all sarih expressions unless specified otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ... وأما الصريح فما ظهر المراد به ظهوراً بينا حقيقة كان أو مجازاً كقوله أنت حر وأنت طالق وحكمه تعلق الحكم بعين الكلام وقيامه مقام معناه حتى استغنى عن العزيمة . صریح کی تعریف : لفظ صریح وہ لفظ ہے جس کی مراد و اضح ہو خواہ وہ حقیقت ہو یا مجاز جیسے انت حر اور انت طالق کیونکہ یہ دونوں رقیت اور نکاح کو زائل کرنے میں صریح ہے صریح کا حکم : اس کا حکم عین کلام کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتا ہے اور اس میں نیت کی بھی ضرورت نہیں ہوتی ما اگر تھا لیکن اس نے یا ہو ہے۔ مثلا اگر کوئی شخص سبحان اللہ کہنا چاہتا تھا لیکن اس نے انت طالق کہد یا طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔
He said: 'As for al-sarih, it is that whose intended meaning is manifestly clear, whether it is literal or figurative, like your saying 'You are free' and 'You are divorced.' Its ruling is that the legal effect is attached to the very wording and it stands in place of its meaning such that it is independent of intention.' Definition of Sarih: Sarih is a word whose intended meaning is clear, whether it is literal or figurative, like 'anti hurr' and 'anti taliq' because both are explicit in removing slavery and marriage. Ruling of Sarih: Its ruling is that it attaches to the very wording and there is no need for intention. For example, if someone intended to say 'Subhanallah' but said 'anti taliq', divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
Here the text explains the concept of sarih (explicit expression). The passage opens with a definition: sarih is a word whose meaning is evident, whether used literally or figuratively. The key example is divorce and emancipation: if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced' (anti taliq), this is sarih because it clearly expresses divorce regardless of his intention. The ruling for sarih is that the legal effect (divorce or emancipation) is attached to the actual utterance, and no intention (niyyah) is required. The author gives an example: if a person intended to say 'Subhanallah' but accidentally said 'anti taliq', divorce occurs because the wording itself is explicit. This is a standard Hanafi position: sarih expressions in divorce and manumission are effective even if the speaker did not intend them, whereas kinayah requires intention. The passage also notes that sarih can be either haqiqah (literal) or majaz (figurative), but in either case the meaning is clear. This lesson is fundamental for understanding the division of expressions in Hanafi fiqh, particularly in the book of divorce (kitab al-talaq).

Key Terms
صریح (ṣarīḥ): A word whose intended meaning is manifestly clear, whether literal or figurative, such that it does not require intention for its legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a man says 'Anti taliq' to his wife but intended to say 'Subhanallah', what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs only if he intended divorce.
B) Divorce does not occur because he had no intention.
C) Divorce occurs because the wording is sarih and does not require intention.
D) It depends on the judge's ruling.

Key Takeaway
Sarih expressions are effective by their wording alone; the speaker's intention does not affect the legal ruling.

Definition and Ruling of Kinayah (Implicit Expression)
Defining kinayah and its ruling that intention is required for legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kinayah in Hanafi usul and state its ruling that legal effect requires intention or a contextual indicator.

Core Idea
Kinayah: a word that needs intention or context to unlock its legal meaning.

In Hanafi usul, expressions are categorized as sarih (explicit) or kinayah (implicit). While sarih words produce legal effect without extra conditions, kinayah words are ambiguous and require either the speaker's intention or a clear contextual clue to determine their intended meaning. This distinction is vital in areas like divorce, where ambiguous words should not inadvertently dissolve a marriage.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read the Arabic definition: kinayah is a word whose intended meaning is hidden and only understood through a qarīnah (contextual indicator). Note that both literal and figurative words can be kinayah if their usage makes the meaning unclear.
The hiddenness is in usage, not in the word itself.
2
Identify the key elements
Break down the definition into its components: lafẓ (utterance), istitār (hiddenness), and qarīnah (indicator). Recognize that the term is defined by its function in communication.
Pronouns like 'anta' are classic examples because they refer to unknown persons without context.
3
Understand the ruling
The ruling is that 'action upon it is not obligatory except with intention.' That is, for a kinayah expression to have legal force (e.g., divorce), the speaker must have intended that meaning, or a clear contextual indicator must stand in for intention.
This protects against accidental legal consequences from careless speech.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
Examine the objection that words like 'anti bā'in' seem obviously divorce-related. The answer explains that they remain kinayah because they can be used figuratively; hence intention is still required to confirm the meaning.
The answer, though partially corrupt in OCR, hinges on the possibility of other meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وأما الكناية فما استتر المراد به ولا يفهم الا بقرينة حقيقة كان أو مجاز أ مثل الفاظ الضمير وحكمها أن لا يجب العمل بها الا با لنية . کنایہ کی تعریف : کنایہ و والفظ ہے جس کی مراد پوشیدہ ہو اور کسی قرینے کے بغیر سمجھ میں نہیں آتی ہو خواہ حقیقت ہو یا مجاز ۔ کنایہ میں جو پوشیدگی ہوتی ہے وہ استعمال کے اعتبارت ہوتی ہے جیسے کہ الفاظ ضمیر ہیں، انسا ، و انت وغیرہ یہ سب اس لئے وضع کئے گئے ہیں تا کہ ان کو متکلم خفاء کے طور پر استعمال کر لے۔ باقی نحویوں کے ہاں ضمیر کا اعرف المعارف ہونا ضروری بات ہے کنایہ کا حکم : اس کا حکم یہ ہے کہ اس پر عمل کر ن اس وقت تک واجب نہیں جب تک اس کے کسی معنی کی تربیت نہ ہو یا نسیت کے قائم مقام کوئی اور چیز نہ ہو۔ قوله : وكنايات الطلاق يت بها مجازاً حتى كانت بو ائن . ایک اہم اعتراض کا دفعیہ : اعتراض : کنایه تو ده و تابے بس کی باشید درون لانك انت بائن ، بتة بتلة، حرام دان رام و غیر وان سب کے معانی تو معلوم ہیں۔ جواب ان الفاظ کو ان یہ کہتا جا تا ہے ۔ کیونکہ مالی بائن کا من ناح کیے۔ معلوم ہے یعنی جدا ہونا کو کن یہ شالا صبح
He said: 'As for al-kinayah, it is that whose intended meaning is hidden and is only understood through a contextual indicator (qarina), whether literal or figurative, like the words of the pronoun. Its ruling is that action upon it is not obligatory except with intention.' Definition of Kinayah: Kinayah is a word whose intended meaning is hidden and is not understood without some contextual indicator, whether literal or figurative. The hiddenness in kinayah is due to usage, such as the words of pronoun: 'anta', 'anti', etc. They were coined so that the speaker may use them in a hidden manner. However, grammarians hold that the pronoun is the most definite of definite things. Ruling of Kinayah: Its ruling is that acting upon it is not obligatory until there is intention (niyyah) for some meaning or something that stands in place of intention. He said: 'And the kinayah of divorce are used figuratively, so they are like bā'in.' An important rebuttal of an objection: Objection: [The words] like 'anti bā'in', 'batta', 'batlah', 'harām', etc., their meanings are known. Answer: These words are considered kinayah because... (text corrupt).
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage explains kinayah (implicit expression) as the counterpart to sarih. The author begins with the definition: kinayah is an expression whose intended meaning is concealed and only understood through a contextual indicator (qarīnah). This hiddenness is not in the word itself but in how it is used. The text gives examples: pronouns like 'anta' and 'anti' are kinayah because they can refer to many different persons depending on context. The ruling for kinayah is that it does not produce legal effect without intention (niyyah) or something that substitutes for it, like a contextual clue indicating the desired meaning. The passage then addresses an important objection regarding divorce kinayah: words like 'anti bā'in' (you are separated) seem to have a clear meaning of divorce, so why are they considered kinayah? The answer (though the OCR is corrupt) is that such words are still kinayah because they can be used figuratively in different contexts, and therefore require intention to specify divorce. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: in divorce, sarih expressions (like 'anti taliq') require no intention, while kinayah expressions require either intention or a clear context. The later Hanafi ruling is that kinayah divorce only takes effect if the husband intended divorce, or if there is a qarīnah indicating divorce (such as during a quarrel). This ensures that mere ambiguous words do not cause divorce unintentionally.

Key Terms
کنایہ (kināyah): A word whose intended meaning is hidden and is understood only through a contextual indicator (qarīnah), and it requires intention for legal effect.
قرینہ (qarīnah): A contextual indicator that clarifies the intended meaning of a kinayah expression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is required for a kinayah expression to produce legal effect?
A) Only the utterance.
B) Intention or a contextual indicator (qarīnah).
C) The presence of a judge.
D) Repetition of the word.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah requires intention or a contextual indicator to produce legal effect; without it, no legal consequence follows.

Understanding Iqtida' al-Nass: The Implicit Requirement of the Text
Defines iqtida' al-nass with two interpretations of the matn's wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define iqtida' al-nass, distinguish between the two interpretations of the matn, and apply the concept to identify an implicit condition required by a text.

Core Idea
Iqtida' al-nass derives a ruling from what the text must require for its own validity, not from explicit words.

This passage introduces a subtle but crucial concept in Hanafi usul: iqtida' al-nass — the implicit requirement of the text. While previous lessons dealt with explicit textual rulings (mantuq) or implied meanings (mafhum), here we encounter a different kind of implication: the text cannot operate without a prior condition. The commentator clarifies two possible interpretations of the author's words, showing the depth of precise usul definitions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the conceptual shift
Understand that iqtida' al-nass is not about what the text says explicitly, but about what it silently requires for its own application. The text only works if a prior condition exists.
This shift from explicit to implicit is key.
2
Break down the lexical terms
Identify the three related terms: iqtida' (the act of requiring), muqtadi (the text that requires), and muqtada (the required condition or ruling). The passage defines the muqtada.
These precise terms prevent confusion.
3
Analyze the first tawjih
According to the first interpretation, the author defined the muqtada itself — the thing that the text needs for its correctness. The text's wording 'ma la ya'malu al-nass illa bi-shart taqaddumihi' is taken as a definition of muqtada.
This changes the author's usual style.
4
Analyze the second tawjih
In the second interpretation, the author defined the ruling (hukm) derived from iqtida' al-nass. The ruling is one where the text operates only with a prior condition, aligning with earlier patterns.
This preserves consistency with previous definitions.
5
Synthesize the concept
Combine both interpretations to grasp iqtida' al-nass: it is a method of deriving a ruling that the text requires for its own validity, with the ruling being conditional on a prior unstated condition.
The practical outcome is that such a ruling is implicit, not explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله اما الثابت باقتضاء النص فما لا يعمل النص الا بشرط تقدمه (۴) استدلال باقتضاء النص تقدمه الخ. لغوی معنی: چیز کا نہ تقاضہ کرے اس مقتضائے نص سے دلیل پکڑنا ۔ الطلاق تعريف: - لا تعمل النص الا بشرط تقدمه چند ضروری اصطلاحات (۱) اقتضاء: اتقاضہ کرنا ( معنی مصدری) (۲) مقتضی تقاضہ کر نے والا (اسم فاعل) (۳) مقتضی ۔ جس معنی / حکم کا مقتدی نے تقاضہ کیا۔ حل عبارت: صاحب کتاب رحمہ اللہ اس مقام پر ایک نسبتاً مشکل اور مغلق عبارت لائے ہیں۔ جو محتاج وضاحت ایک ہے اور شارح علام رحمتہ اللہ علیہ نے اس عبارت کو حل کرتے ہوئے دو تو جیہیں بیان کی ہیں ۔ جو کتاب میں شرح وبسط کے ساتھ مذکور ہیں۔ پہلی توجیہ: اس توجیہ کے مطابق صاحب کتاب رحمتہ اللہ علیہ نے مقتضی کی تعریف کی ہے، کہتے ہیں: مخفی وہ ہے جس ہوا اس نے تقاضہ اس چیز کی درستگی کے لئے کیا ، جس کو نصی شامل ہے " عبارت میں اقتضاء مصدر اپنے معنی میں مستعمل ہے اور مائن کا قول "مقتضی " بمعنی اقتنا، ہے اس عبارت کا حاصل اور خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ یہاں نفس مقتضی کی تعریف کی گئی ہے اور اس حکم کی تعریف نہیں جو مقتضی سے ثابت ہے ۔ الغرض مصنف رحمہ اللہ نے اپنا اسلوب بدل دیا ہے اور بجائے اقتضاء النص سے ثابت ہونے والے حکم کی تعریف کے نفس مخصی کی تعریف کی ہے۔ جبکہ ماقبل میں حکم کی تعریف کر تے تھے ۔ دوسری توجیه: اس صورت میں ماتن رحمہ اللہ کی عبارت اس حکم کی تعریف ہے جو متن تمنائے اس سے مستفاد ہوتا ہے ۔ وہ حکم جو اس سے مشخصی سے ثابت ہوتا ہے اس میں نص فقط ایک شرط کے ساتھ عمل کرتی ہے ه بيه که شهر این مقنی (جس کا اس نے اپنے مفہوم کی صحت کے لئے القانہ کیا ہے ) نص سے
He said: As for that which is established by requirement of the text (iqtida' al-nass), it is that which the text does not operate except with a prior condition. (4) Istidlal bi iqtida' al-nass. Lexical meaning: To take evidence from the requirement of the text [clarify]. At-talaq [stray word?] Definition: It is that which the text does not operate except with a prior condition. Necessary terms: (1) Iqtida': to require (verbal noun). (2) Muqtadi: the one who requires (active participle). (3) Muqtada: the meaning/ruling that the muqtadi required. Resolution of the phrase: The author (ra) brought a relatively difficult and ambiguous expression here, which needs clarification, and the commentator (ra) gave two explanations in resolving it, which are mentioned with detail in the book. First explanation: According to this, the author (ra) defined muqtada (the required thing). He says: 'Muqtada is that which its [the text's] requirement is for the correctness of that which the text includes.' In the phrase, iqtida' is used as a verbal noun meaning its own meaning, and the author's word 'muqtada' is in the sense of iqtida'. The summary is that here the muqtada itself is defined, not the ruling established by the muqtada. In short, the author changed his style and instead of defining the ruling derived from iqtida' al-nass, he defined the muqtada itself, whereas before he used to define the ruling. Second explanation: In this case, the author's phrase is a definition of the ruling that is derived from the text. That ruling which is established from iqtida' al-nass is such that the text only operates with a condition...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the concept of iqtida' al-nass (requirement of the text), a source of ruling in Hanafi usul. The matn states that a ruling derived by iqtida' al-nass is one where the text only operates with a prior condition (i.e., the text implies something necessary for its validity). The commentator first gives lexical meanings of iqtida', muqtadi, and muqtada. Then he discusses two interpretations of the matn: (1) The author actually defined the muqtada itself (the required thing), not the ruling; (2) Alternatively, the author's phrase is a definition of the ruling established by iqtida' al-nass, i.e., a ruling that requires a prior condition for the text's operation. The second interpretation aligns with the pattern of earlier definitions. The passage is typical of usul al-fiqh where precise definitions are crucial.

Key Terms
اقتضاء النص (iqtida' al-nass): A method of deriving a ruling from the text by assuming a necessary condition that the text implies for its own validity.
مقتضی (muqtada): The thing (meaning or ruling) that the text requires for its correctness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The ruling (hukm) derived from iqtida' al-nass.
B) The muqtada (the required thing) itself.
C) The definition of 'nass'.
D) The condition (shart) of the text.

Key Takeaway
Iqtida' al-nass derives an implicit condition necessary for the text's validity, not an explicit ruling.

Muqtada vs Mahdhuf: The Sign of Implication in Hanafi Usul
Defining the sign of muqtada and distinguishing it from mahdhuf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muqtada and mahdhuf by applying the sign of non-change in meaning and syntax when the implied element is made explicit.

Core Idea
Muqtada does not alter a text when stated explicitly; mahdhuf does — this sign reveals whether an omitted element is a necessary implication or mere linguistic ellipsis.

In Hanafi usul, understanding the difference between a necessary implication (muqtada) and a linguistic omission (mahdhuf) is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts. This lesson clarifies the defining sign of muqtada: if you state the implied element explicitly, the meaning and grammatical structure remain unchanged. We will also contrast it with mahdhuf, where restoration causes change, using classical examples from juristic statements and the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of muqtada and its sign
Read the passage to find the sign of muqtada: when the implied element is made explicit, the expression does not change in meaning or syntax.
The sign is the key differentiator from mahdhuf.
2
Examine the example of muqtada (the vow)
In the vow 'If I eat, then my slave is free,' the word 'food' is necessarily implied. Stating it explicitly does not alter the ruling; thus it is muqtada.
Note that the implied element is necessary for the statement to be valid in shariah or reason.
3
Examine the example of mahdhuf (Quranic verse)
In 'Ask the village,' the word 'people' is omitted. Restoring it changes the meaning (from the place to the inhabitants) and syntax (case changes from accusative to genitive). Hence it is mahdhuf.
Mahdhuf is for brevity and its restoration alters the text.
4
Summarize the three differences
The passage lists three differences: (1) effect on expression when restored, (2) basis (shariah/reason vs. language), (3) purpose (making sense vs. brevity).
Memorize these to quickly distinguish the two.
5
Apply the sign to a new text
Given a new legal statement, test whether restoring an implied element changes meaning/syntax. If not, it is muqtada; if yes, it is mahdhuf or another type.
This is the practical skill for reading legal texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقتضیٰ اور محذوف کا فرق:
ماتن رحمہ اللہ نے سب پہلے مقتضیٰ کی علامت کو مثال کے ذریعہ واضح فرمایا ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ
مقتضی کی علامت: مقتضیٰ اگر کسی وقت عبارت میں ظاہر بھی کر دیا جائے تو اس کے ظہور سے عبارت میں کسی قسم کا تغیر واقع نہیں ہوتا ہے۔
مثال سے وضاحت: قاتل کا یہ قول : "ان اکلت فعبدی حر بطریق اقتضاء النص طعاماً" مقتضی کا تقاضا کرتا ہے۔ اگر اسے عبارت میں ظاہر کر کے یوں کہا جائے "ان اکلت الطعام فعبدى حر" تو بھی حکم میں کسی قسم کی تبدیلی نہیں آتی۔ اس سے مقتضیٰ اور محذوف کا فرق بھی معلوم ہو گیا کہ مقتضیٰ کو اگر عبارت میں ظاہر بھی کر دیا جائے تو عبارت متغیر نہیں ہوتی جبکہ محذوف کو اگر ظاہر کر دیا جائے تو عبارت میں نہ صرف باعتبار ترکیب بلکہ مقصد و مراد کے اعتبار سے بھی تبدیلی واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔ جیسے اس مثال سے سمجھا جا سکتا ہے۔
محذوف کی مثال سے وضاحت:
اللہ تعالیٰ کا قول ہے " واسئل القريه " اگر ہم محذوف (اصل) کو ظاہر کر کے یوں پڑھیں: "واسنل اهل القريه" تو ملاحظہ فرمائیے عبارت میں دو اعتبار سے تغیر واقع ہوا۔ جس کی وضاحت مندرجہ ذیل ہے:
معنوی تغیر: پہلے سوال بستی سے کرنے کا حکم تھا۔ اب بستی والوں سے ہے یعنی مضاف کو ظاہر کرنے سے مسئول عنہ تبدیل ہو گیا۔
ترکیبی (لفظی) تغیر: پہلے لفظ القریہ پر واسئل فضل امر کا مفعول نصب تھا، اب چونکہ یہ اصل مفعول بہ کے لئے مضاف الیہ واقع ہو رہا ہے لہذا مجرور ہوگا۔
خلاصہ کلام: مقتضیٰ اور محذوف میں مندرجہ ذیل فرق ہیں۔
مقتضی (1) مقتضی کو ظاہر کرنے سے عبارت میں معنوی اور ترکیبی اعتبار سے کوئی تبدیلی نہیں آتی (۲) مقتضی کا تعلق شریعت سے یا عقل سے ہوتا ہے اور اسکا لغت سے کوئی تعلق نہیں ہوتا (۳) مقصیٰ عبارت کو شرعاً یا عقلاً بامعنی بنانے کے لئے لایا جاتا ہے۔
محذوف (2) محذوف کو ظاہر کرنے سے عبارت میں دونوں طرح کی تبدیلیاں آتی ہیں۔ (۲) محذوف کا تعلق لغت سے ہوتا ہے (۳) محذوف عبارت میں اختصار کیلئے لایا جاتا ہے۔
Difference between muqtada and mahdhuf:
The author (rahimahullah) first clarified the sign of muqtada with an example, the summary of which is:
Sign of muqtada: If muqtada is made apparent in the expression at any time, no change occurs in the expression due to its appearance.
Clarification with example: The statement of the killer [in a vow]: "If I eat, then my slave is free" by way of implication of the text (muqtada) requires [the word] "food". If one makes it apparent in the expression and says: "If I eat food, then my slave is free", no change occurs in the ruling. From this, the difference between muqtada and mahdhuf is also known: if muqtada is made apparent in the expression, the expression does not change, whereas if mahdhuf is made apparent, change occurs in the expression not only in terms of composition but also in terms of intent and meaning. As can be understood from this example.
Clarification with example of mahdhuf:
Allah Most High says: "And ask the village" (Quran 12:82). If we make the mahdhuf (original) apparent and read: "And ask the people of the village", then observe that change occurs in the expression in two respects:
Semantic change: Previously, the command was to ask the village; now it is to ask the people of the village, i.e., by making the mudaf apparent, the object of inquiry changed.
Syntactic (word-form) change: Previously, the word "al-qaryah" was in the accusative case as the object of the imperative "ask"; now, since it is the mudaf ilayh for the actual object, it will be in the genitive case.
Summary: The differences between muqtada and mahdhuf are:
Muqtada: (1) Making muqtada apparent causes no change in meaning or composition. (2) Muqtada is related to shariah or reason, and has no relation to language. (3) Muqtada is brought to make the expression meaningful according to shariah or reason.
Mahdhuf: (2) Making mahdhuf apparent causes both kinds of changes. (2) Mahdhuf is related to language. (3) Mahdhuf is brought for brevity in expression.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a definition and comparison of two key concepts in Hanafi usul: muqtada (implied element) and mahdhuf (omitted element). The author first explains the sign (علامة) of muqtada: if the implied element is explicitly stated, the meaning and syntax of the sentence remain unchanged. He illustrates with a vow: if someone says 'If I eat, then my slave is free,' the word 'food' is necessarily implied (muqtada) because whether one eats food or something else is irrelevant. Adding 'food' does not alter the ruling. In contrast, mahdhuf is an omitted element that, when restored, changes both meaning and syntax. The Quranic example 'Ask the village' is cited: the word 'people' is omitted (mahdhuf). Restoring it changes the object from the village (place) to its inhabitants (people), and the grammatical case of 'al-qaryah' shifts from accusative (مفعول) to genitive (مضاف إليه). The author then summarizes three differences: (1) effect of restoration, (2) basis of necessity (shariah/reason vs. language), and (3) purpose (making sense vs. brevity). This distinction is fundamental in interpreting legal texts: muqtada is necessary for the statement to be valid in shariah or reason, while mahdhuf is a linguistic ellipsis for conciseness.

Key Terms
مقتضی (muqtadā): An element necessarily implied by a text such that the text would be invalid in shariah or reason without it; making it explicit does not change the text's meaning or syntax.
محذوف (maḥdhūf): An element omitted from a text for brevity; restoring it changes both the meaning and syntactic structure of the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the meaning and syntax of a statement if the muqtada is made explicit?
A) Both meaning and syntax change.
B) Only meaning changes.
C) Only syntax changes.
D) Neither meaning nor syntax changes.

Key Takeaway
Muqtada is a necessary implication that does not alter a text when made explicit, while mahdhuf is a linguistic omission that does alter the text when restored.

Engaging in the Opposite of a Commanded Act: Hanafi Analysis
Concludes discussion on didd principle with two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that engaging in the opposite of a commanded act can invalidate or undermine the commanded act, using the examples of prayer on najis and muhrim's clothing.

Core Idea
The opposite of a commanded act is not neutral; it can invalidate or reinforce the commanded act.

This passage concludes a usul al-fiqh discussion on a critical question: when a person engages in the opposite (didd) of a commanded act, does that strengthen the commanded act or nullify it? The author presents two Hanafi examples to illustrate the principle. The first example involves prayer on a najis place, where a difference of opinion exists between Imam Abu Yusuf and the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). The second example involves a muhrim avoiding sewn clothes, where the opposite action (wearing a sheet and waist-wrapper) is effectively mandatory. By studying these examples, you will learn how the Hanafi school applies the concept of 'continuous obligation' (farz da'imi) to determine the validity of an act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commanded act and its opposite
In each example, determine what act is commanded (ma'mur bihi) and what its direct opposite (didd) is. For example, in prayer, the commanded act includes valid prayer with purity; the opposite is prostrating on najis.
2
Analyze the first example: prayer on najis
Compare the positions of Abu Yusuf and the two. Abu Yusuf holds that repeating the prostration on pure ground saves the prayer; the two hold that purification is a continuous obligation (farz da'imi) and any najis prostration instantly invalidates.
The key usul principle is whether an obligation can be compensated later or must be fulfilled at every moment.
3
Extract the underlying usul principle
The dispute hinges on the nature of the obligation of purity in prayer: is it a continuous obligation (cannot be missed at any moment) or a cumulative obligation that can be made up? The two's view aligns with the first, Abu Yusuf with the second.
4
Apply the principle to the second example: ihram clothing
Here, the commanded act is avoiding sewn clothes; the opposite is wearing a sheet and waist-wrapper. The text states that this opposite is so essential that it is like a confirmed sunnah, i.e., it must be done. This shows that engaging in the opposite can strengthen the commanded act.
The second example illustrates that the opposite can be mandatory, reinforcing the commanded act.
5
Conclude the tarjih
The author ends by dismissing weak arguments and siding with the two. The relied-upon position is that engaging in the opposite of a commanded act, when the opposite is a continuous obligation, invalidates the commanded act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہے کہ مامور بہ کی ضد میں مشغولیت مامور بہ کی تقویت کو مستلزم ہے یا نہیں ۔ امام ابو یوسف رحمہ اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مکان نجس پر سجدہ کے بعد چونکہ اس نے مکان طاہر پر اس سجدہ کا اعادہ کیا ہے لہذا مامور به فوت نہیں ہوا۔ زیادہ سے زیادہ اس نے ایک مکروہ فعل کا ارتکاب کیا وهو لا يـوجـب فساد الصلوة - جبکہ طرفین رحمھما اللہ کے ہاں مکان نجس میں سجدہ کرتے ہی مامور به فوت ہو گیا۔ کیونکہ نماز میں تطہیر ایک دائمی فرض ہے۔ اور مکان نجس پر سجدہ کرنے والا حامل نجاست ہے لہذا مکان نجس پر سجدہ حرام ہے جو فرض تطہیر کو فوت کر رہا ہے اور فرض رہ جانے سے نماز فوت ہو جاتی ہے۔
ثانی کی مثال محرم کا سلے ہوئے کپڑے نہ پہننا ہے کہ اس منہی عنہ کی ضد یعنی چادر اور تہبند وغیرہ کو حالت احرام میں ترک نہیں کیا جائے گا جس طرح سنت مؤکدہ کو ترک نہیں کیا جاتا۔ ط تمسکات ضعیفه و فاسده تمام شد
Whether being engaged in the opposite of a commanded act necessitates the strengthening of the commanded act or not. Imam Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, says that after prostrating on a najis place, since he repeated that prostration on a pure place, therefore the commanded act was not missed. At most he committed a disliked act, and it does not invalidate the prayer. Whereas according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), may Allah have mercy on them, as soon as he prostrated on the najis place, the commanded act was lost. Because purification in prayer is a continuous obligation. And the one who prostrates on a najis place is carrying impurity, therefore prostrating on a najis place is forbidden, which causes the loss of the obligation of purification, and with the obligation remaining unfulfilled, the prayer is lost. The second example is that of the muhrim not wearing sewn clothes: the opposite of this prohibited thing, i.e., a sheet and waist-wrapper etc., will not be abandoned in the state of ihram, just as a confirmed sunnah is not abandoned. Weak and corrupt arguments ended.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage concludes a discussion on a usul al-fiqh principle: whether engaging in the opposite of a commanded act (didd) can strengthen the commanded act itself. The author presents two examples from Hanafi fiqh to illustrate the difference of opinion. The first example: praying on a najis place. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that if one prostrates on a najis place and later repeats the prostration on a pure place within the same prayer, the commanded act (valid prayer) is not lost; the repetition fulfills it. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that purification is a continuous obligation in prayer; prostrating on najis is haram and causes the obligation to be missed, thus invalidating the prayer. The second example: a muhrim must avoid sewn clothes; the opposite (wearing a sheet and waist-wrapper) is obligatory to the extent that it is like a confirmed sunnah that cannot be abandoned. The author ends by dismissing weak and corrupt arguments, indicating that the correct Hanafi position is the one that considers engagement in the opposite as strengthening the commanded act in certain contexts.

Key Terms
مامور به (ma'mur bihi): The act that is commanded by Shariah, e.g., valid prayer.
منہی عنہ (manhi 'anhu): The act that is prohibited by Shariah, e.g., wearing sewn clothes in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a person prostrates on a najis place during prayer?
A) The prayer is valid if the prostration is later repeated on a pure place.
B) The prayer is invalid because purification is a continuous obligation.
C) The prayer is makruh but valid.
D) The prayer is invalid only if the impurity was intentional.

Key Takeaway
Engaging in the opposite of a commanded act can either strengthen or invalidate the commanded act, depending on whether the obligation is continuous or can be compensated.

Rukhsah Majaziyyah: Definition and Subtypes
Defines rukhsah majaziyyah and its two subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define rukhsah majaziyyah and distinguish its two subtypes (atamm and naqis) based on the degree to which the original azimah is lost.

Core Idea
Rukhsah majaziyyah is actually a new azimah, metaphorically called a license because the original resolve is lost.

In Hanafi usul, rulings are divided into azimah (original, general ruling) and rukhsah (exceptional license). This lesson clarifies the second type of rukhsah: the metaphorical license (rukhsah majaziyyah). Unlike genuine licenses that relax the rule, this type is itself a binding obligation—yet it is called a license because the original ruling no longer applies. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between different kinds of licenses and is essential for proper categorization of legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its literal vs metaphorical usage
Note that 'rukhsah majaziyyah' is called 'license' only metaphorically; its reality is a new azimah. This is because the original azimah has been lost, so the replacement ruling becomes the new default.
2
Understand the cause: loss of azimah
The sabab for calling it a metaphorical license is the loss (fawat) of the original azimah. Without this loss, the ruling would remain azimah.
3
Differentiate the two subtypes: atamm and naqis
Atamm: azimah is lost completely from all aspects (min kull al-wujuh). Naqis: azimah remains in some aspects (ba'd al-wujuh). This distinction affects the degree of metaphoricity.
4
Apply the definition to a concrete scenario
Consider a ruling where a condition is permanently removed (e.g., water impossibility making tayammum obligatory). Determine which subtype applies based on whether any aspect of the original azimah remains.
This step reinforces the practical identification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) رخصت مجازیه ایسی رخصت جسمیں عزیمت فوت ہو جائے یعنی معمول بہار ہے اس قسم کو مجازا رخواست کہا گیا ۔ ور نہ حقیقتاً یہ خود عزیمت ہے لفوات العزيمة پھر اگر عـزيـمـت من كل الوجوه جميع عالم سے فوت ہے تو یہ اتم انجاز ہے اور اگر بعض مادوں کے اندر عزیمت موجود ہے تو یہ مجاز ہونے میں غیر تام اور ناقص ہے۔
(2) Legitimized license (rukhsah majaziyyah): A license in which the resolve (azimah) is lost, i.e., the practiced ruling; this type is called license metaphorically, otherwise in reality it is itself resolve, due to the loss of resolve. Then if the resolve is lost from all aspects completely, then it is the most perfect (atamm) [type of] license, and if the resolve exists in some aspects, then it is incomplete and deficient (naqis) in being metaphorical.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of license (rukhsah), called 'legitimized license' (rukhsah majaziyyah). It explains that this type is actually a resolve (azimah) but is called license because the original resolve is no longer applicable. It then divides this into two subtypes: the perfect kind (atamm) where the resolve is completely absent in all respects, and the imperfect kind (naqis) where some aspects of the resolve remain. The author highlights that this classification is based on the degree to which the original ruling (azimah) is abandoned.

Key Terms
رخصت (rukhṣah): A simplified or exceptional ruling due to a valid excuse, opposed to azimah.
عزیمت (ʿazīmah): The original, general ruling without any exception.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical usage; here, calling something rukhsah although it is actually azimah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In rukhsah majaziyyah, what distinguishes the 'atamm' (perfect) subtype from the 'naqis' (deficient) subtype?
A) Atamm applies only in travel, while naqis applies in illness.
B) In atamm, the original azimah is completely lost in all aspects; in naqis, some aspects of azimah remain.
C) Atamm is stronger in evidence, naqis is weaker.
D) Atamm is for compulsory acts, naqis for voluntary acts.

Key Takeaway
Rukhsah majaziyyah is a metaphorical license: it is actually a new resolve (azimah) that replaces the original when the original is no longer applicable.

First Type of Genuine License (Rukhsah Haqiqiyyah) in Usul al-Fiqh
Explains the ruling and examples of the first type of genuine license.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that in genuine license (rukhsah haqiqiyyah) the original prohibition remains but accountability is waived, and identify the remaining right in each scenario.

Core Idea
A genuine license keeps the prohibition intact but suspends accountability.

In this lesson, we examine the first type of genuine license as defined by Hanafi usul scholars. The key distinction is that the prohibited act remains forbidden in essence, but the person is excused from punishment due to necessity. The passage provides six classic examples, each illustrating how one right (of Allah or of a person) persists even when the license is invoked.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general ruling
State that acting on the original rule (azimah) is superior, but acting on the license is also permissible.
2
Identify the legal reason (ta'lil)
The prohibited element and its cause remain, but accountability is lifted.
The act does not become halal; the person is treated as if they did something permissible only regarding accountability.
3
Distinguish from making the act halal
The license does not change the inherent prohibition; it only removes punishment.
This is a critical distinction: rukhsah haqiqiyyah differs from rukhsah that changes the ruling itself.
4
Examine the examples
For each of the six examples, note the scenario and the specific right that remains.
The rights are: inner conviction, making up a fast, compensation, belief in prohibition, expiatory penalty, and guarantee.
5
Synthesize the common pattern
In every example, one right (haqq) of Allah or of a person remains after applying the license.
This pattern defines the first type of genuine license.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اما احق نوعى الحقيقة. رخصت حقیقیہ کی نوع کے بعض احکام اس قسم کا حکم یہ ہے کہ عزیمت پر عمل کرنا اولی ہے لیکن رخصت پر عمل بھی جائز ہے کیونکہ جانب رخصت میں محرم اور اس کا موجب باقی ہیں لیکن مواخذہ کے حق میں باوجود دلیل حرمت قائم ہونے کے اس کے مرتکب کے ساتھ مباح کی طرح معاملہ ہے۔ یہ مطلب نہیں کہ فی نفسہ یہ افعال حرام نہ رہے بلکہ مباح ہو گئے ۔ بلکہ یہ تمثیل محض ترک مواخذہ کے حق میں ہے۔ شارخ نے مصنف رحمہ اللہ کی بیان کردہ ہر مثال کی سیر حاصل وضاحت کی ہے ہم صرف امثلہ کے فقل پر اکتفاء کرتے ہیں۔ رخصت حقیقیہ کی قسم اول کی مثالیں:۔ (۱) حالت اکراد میں کلمہ کفر کہنا جبکہ دل مطمئن بالایمان ہو (۲) حالت اکراہ میں روزہ فرض افتخار کرنا (۳) حالت اگر اہ میں دوسرے کے مال یا نفس کو تلف کرنا (۴) ظالم جابر بادشاہ سے جان کا خوف ہونے کی بنا پر امر بالمعروف اور نہی عن المنکر کا ترک کرنا۔ (۵) مجبور کا حالت احرام میں کی نیت کا ارتکاب کرنا (۶) حالت مخمصہ میں مال غیر کھانا۔ نکتہ : ۔ ۔ ۔ ۔ ۔ تمام مثالوں پر غور کرنے پر معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ عز نیت کی صورت میں بالکلیہ اس کا حق فوت ہوتا ہے صورة و معنی جبکہ جانب رخصت میں ایک حق باقی ہے۔ على الترتيب مثال اول میں اللہ کا حق یعنی تصدیق قلبی مثال ثانی میں اللہ کا حق یعنی قضاء صوم، مثال ثالث میں مالک مال کا حق یعنی مہمان ، مثال رابع میں اللہ کا حق یعنی امر بالمعروف نہی عن المنکر کے ترک کی حرمت کا اعتقاد ، مثال خامس میں اللہ کا حق بصورت جزاء تاوان اور مثال سادس میں غیر کا حق ضمان باقی رہتا ہے۔ نوٹ :۔۔۔۔۔ چونکہ یہاں باوجود دلیل حرمت قائم ہونے کے ہندہ کے ساتھ خاص رعایت ندہ کے ساتھ خاص رعا ہے لہذا اس قسم کو رخصت کہنا احق واولی ہے۔
Now the more deserving type of genuine (license). Some rulings of the type of genuine license: The ruling of this type is that acting upon the resolve (azimah) is superior, but acting upon the license is also permissible, because in the aspect of license the prohibited (muharram) and its cause remain, but regarding accountability, despite the evidence of prohibition being established, the one who commits it is treated like one who does something permissible. This does not mean that these acts are no longer prohibited in themselves and become permissible. Rather, this analogy is only regarding the abandonment of accountability. The commentator (Sharikh) has explained each example given by the author (Musannif) in detail; we suffice with just the examples. Examples of the first type of genuine license: (1) Uttering a word of disbelief under coercion while the heart is at peace with faith. (2) Breaking an obligatory fast under coercion. (3) Destroying another's property or life under coercion. (4) Abandoning commanding good and forbidding evil due to fear of death from an oppressive ruler. (5) A compelled person committing a prohibited act during ihram. (6) Eating another's property in a state of starvation. Note: Upon reflection on all examples, it is clear that in the case of resolution (azimah), the right is completely lost in form and meaning, while in the aspect of license, one right remains. In order: In the first example, Allah's right - inner conviction; second, Allah's right - making up the fast; third, the owner's right - compensation; fourth, Allah's right - the belief in the prohibition of abandoning commanding good; fifth, Allah's right - in the form of expiatory penalty; sixth, the other's right - guarantee remains. Note: Since here, despite the evidence of prohibition being established, special consideration is given to the person, therefore calling this type a license is more appropriate and primary.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage explains the first type of genuine license (rukhsah haqiqiyyah). The ruling: acting on the original ruling (azimah) is superior, but the license is also valid. The reason is that the prohibited element and its cause remain in the license, but the person is treated as if they did something permissible in terms of accountability. This is only about accountability, not about the act itself becoming permissible. The author then lists six examples, each illustrating a situation where under duress or necessity, an act normally prohibited becomes permissible, but some right (of Allah or of a person) remains. For each example, the specific right that remains is pointed out. The note emphasizes that because special consideration is given to the person despite the prohibition, this type is rightly called a license.

Key Terms
رخصت حقیقیہ (rukhṣah ḥaqīqīyyah): A genuine license where the original prohibition remains but accountability is waived for a valid excuse.
عزیمت (ʿazīmah): The original ruling that is superior to follow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are equally recommended.
B) Azimah is superior, but rukhsah is permissible.
C) Rukhsah is superior.
D) Neither is valid.

Key Takeaway
In genuine license (rukhsah haqiqiyyah), the act remains prohibited in itself, but the doer is excused from punishment due to necessity; one right (haqq) always remains.

The Perfect Type of Legitimized License (Rukhsah Majaziyyah Atamm)
Describes the atamm subtype of legitimized license and its ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the atamm type of rukhsah majaziyyah and explain how it functions as an azimah for this Ummah while being called a license metaphorically.

Core Idea
The perfect type of legitimized license is actually the azimah for this Ummah, called a license only metaphorically.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, licenses (rukhas) are generally concessions that deviate from the original rule due to necessity. However, one subtype—the atamm (perfect) type of legitimized license—is not a true concession for the Ummah of Muhammad. Rather, it is the standard obligation (azimah) that was made easy compared to the stricter rulings of previous nations. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Term and Its Components
Break down 'rukhsah majaziyyah atamm' into its parts: rukhsah (license), majaziyyah (metaphorical/licit), and atamm (perfect/most complete). Understand that 'majaziyyah' indicates it is not a true license but called so metaphorically.
The term 'atamm' emphasizes that this subtype is the highest form of metaphorical license.
2
Understand the Metaphorical Naming
Explain why this type is called a 'license' metaphorically: it alleviates the harshness of previous shariahs, but for the Ummah of Muhammad, it is the actual obligation (azimah).
The metaphor lies in the comparison with earlier, stricter rulings, not in any leniency for this Ummah.
3
Relate to Previous Shariahs
Recognize that this ruling was a concession for earlier communities, but for Muslims, it is the standard rule. Contrast with real licenses (rukhsah haqiqiyyah) that are concessions for this Ummah.
For example, the permissibility of eating certain foods that were forbidden to Bani Israel is a rukhsah majaziyyah for Muslims.
4
Derive the Ruling for the Ummah
Conclude that for the Ummah of Muhammad, acting upon this ruling is not a concession but the required azimah. It is wrong to treat it as optional.
This has practical implications: a Muslim must follow this rule as an obligation, not as a mere allowance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اما اتم نوعى المجاز: رخصت مجازیہ کی نوع اتم کے چند احکام :۔ اسے مجاز ارخصت کہا گیا ہے حالانکہ یہیں امت محمدیہ علی صاحبھا الصلوۃ والسلام کے حق میں عزیمت ہے۔ اس کا تعلق اہم سابقہ پر مشروع احکام شاقہ سے ہے۔
Now the perfect type of metaphorical (license): Some rulings of the perfect type of legitimized license: It has been called a license metaphorically, although it is the very resolve (azimah) for the Ummah of Muhammad, may peace be upon him. It relates to the harsh rulings legislated upon previous communities.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This brief passage explains the perfect (atamm) subtype of legitimized license (rukhsah majaziyyah). The ruling: this type is actually the azimah (obligation) for this Ummah, but it is called a license metaphorically because it relates to the stricter rulings that were imposed on previous nations. For example, the permissibility of eating certain foods or leniency in some acts that were forbidden to earlier communities. The author emphasizes that for Muslims, this is the standard ruling, not a concession.

Key Terms
رخصت مجازیہ (rukhṣah majāziyyah): A license that is actually an azimah for this Ummah but called license metaphorically because it alleviates the harshness of previous shariahs.
عزیمت (ʿazīmah): The original, obligatory ruling; the opposite of rukhsah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is a recommended act.
D) It is prohibited.

Key Takeaway
The atamm type of rukhsah majaziyyah is not a concession but the required obligation (azimah) for the Ummah of Muhammad; it is called a license only metaphorically because it contrasts with stricter rulings of past nations.

Fourth Type of Rukhsah: Rulings that Fall Away Due to Circumstances
Classifying the fourth type of rukhsah with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth type of rukhsah and explain how qasr al-salah exemplifies it, applying the usul principle of circumstance-based abrogation.

Core Idea
A ruling can be legislated for normal circumstances yet fall away when a specific obstacle arises, such as qasr during travel.

In usul al-fiqh, licenses (rukhas) are categorized. The fourth type is unique: it is a ruling that is legislated for normal situations but becomes inapplicable when a specific circumstance arises. The example of shortening the prayer during travel illustrates how a circumstance (safar) causes the original ruling (full prayer) to give way to a license. Understanding this category helps distinguish between permanent and conditional rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of rukhsah
Recognize that this fourth type describes rulings that are valid in normal conditions but are dropped due to specific circumstances (عوارض).
Note that the ruling does not cease to exist; it is simply suspended for the duration of the circumstance.
2
State the example given
The text gives qasr al-salah (shortening the four-rakat prayers) during travel as an example.
3
Apply the usul principle
The original ruling (full prayer) is replaced by the license (qasr) due to the circumstance (travel). In Hanafi usul, this is a case of rukhsah originating from 'azimah (the original ruling) but suspended by a valid excuse.
Hanafi principle: Rukhsah is the relaxation of the original ruling, but here the original ruling itself is made conditional.
4
Note the epistemological status
The text states there is no disagreement on the permissibility of qasr. This indicates consensus (ijma) on its licitness. However, the ruling's obligation level is debated among madhahib.
In Hanafi fiqh, qasr is wajib; in Shafi'i, it is permissible but not required.
5
Derive the ruling for a traveler
Applying the concept: for a traveler, the full four-rakat prayer is replaced by two rakats. This is the operative ruling in Hanafi fiqh.
The license applies to obligatory prayers of four rakats; maghrib and fajr are not shortened.

The Effect of Necessity on Legal Rulings: Azimah and Rukhsah
Concluding examples on ahkam shar'iyyah: oath-breaking and necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that necessity lifts prohibition entirely, distinguishing it from the view that prohibition remains in essence, and explain the relationship between azimah and rukhsah.

Core Idea
Necessity removes prohibition completely; original ruling (azimah) remains valid but is not applicable during concession.

This passage presents two concluding examples from the discussion of ahkam shar'iyyah. The first concerns an oath to avoid haram meat: if one eats it out of necessity, the school of Imam Abu Hanifa holds that no sin or oath-breaking occurs because the prohibition itself is lifted. The second example shows that wiping over khuffs is a concession that abrogates the obligation to wash the feet, while the original ruling remains intact. Both illustrate the Hanafi distinction between azimah (original ruling) and rukhsah (concession) and how necessity temporarily overrides prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal scenarios
The passage gives two cases: (1) eating haram meat under necessity after swearing not to, and (2) wiping over khuffs instead of washing feet. These are concrete applications of usul principles.
The first involves an oath, the second involves an alternative act in wudu.
2
Analyze the Hanafi position on necessity in the oath case
Hanafi view: necessity wholly lifts the prohibition of the haram meat, so the act is not haram during the state of necessity. Therefore, breaking the oath does not occur because the act was not actually haram at the time.
The text says: 'because in a state of necessity, the prohibition of those things has fallen away'.
3
Contrast with the opposing view on the oath case
The opposing school (likely Shafi'i) holds that the essence of prohibition remains even under necessity; only the sin is removed. Hence, the act is still considered haram in essence, so the oath is broken.
This difference stems from how necessity is understood: does it remove the ruling (hukm) or just the liability?
4
Understand azimah vs rukhsah via the khuff example
Wiping over khuffs is a rukhsah (concession) that makes washing the feet non-obligatory. However, the azimah (original ruling) of washing the feet remains as the baseline. This shows that a rukhsah does not abrogate the azimah but provides an alternative.
The text cites Sahib al-Hidayah: 'washing the feet is the original ruling (azimah)'.
5
Derive the usul principle
From these examples, we derive that in Hanafi usul, necessity (idtarar) temporarily removes prohibitions entirely, and concessions (rukhsah) are valid alternatives that do not negate the original ruling (azimah). This reinforces the Hanafi approach of balancing strictness and flexibility.
The principle is applied in many chapters: eating dead meat, drinking wine, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حرام گوشت کھالے تو ان کے ہاں چونکہ نفس حرمت باقی ہے لہذا حانث ہوگا ۔ اور ہمارے ہاں نہیں امام ہوگا۔ کیونکہ حالت اضطرار میں ان چیزوں کی حرمت ساقط ہو چکی ہے
تیسری مثال :---- مسح على الخفين کرنے سے غسل رجل کی فرضیت ساقط ہے
صاحب ہدایہ کے ہاں غسل رجل ہی عزیمت ہے۔
احکام مشروعیہ کی بحث مکمل ہوئی
If one eats haram meat, then according to them (the opponents), because the essence of prohibition remains, therefore he would be guilty of breaking the oath. And according to us, he would not be (the Imam holds). Because in a state of necessity, the prohibition of those things has fallen away. Third example: Wiping over the khuffs causes the obligation of washing the feet to drop. According to Sahib al-Hidayah, washing the feet is the original ruling. The discussion of legal rulings is concluded.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of ahkam shar'iyyah with two examples. The first concerns an oath: if someone swears not to eat haram meat but then eats it under necessity, the opponent school (likely Shafi'i) holds that since the prohibition remains in essence (even if temporarily lifted), the oath is broken. The Hanafi view, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, is that necessity removes the prohibition entirely, so no sin or oath-breaking occurs. The second example relates to wudu: wiping over khuffs (khuffain) is an alternative to washing the feet; it abrogates the obligation of washing the feet as a concession (rukhsah), but the original ruling (azimah) remains washing the feet, as per the Hidayah. This underscores the Hanafi principle that concessions do not negate the original ruling.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by doing what one swore not to do.
عزیمت (azimah): The original, uncompromised ruling before any concession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented, why does eating haram meat under necessity not break an oath?
A) Because the oath was invalid
B) Because the prohibition is lifted entirely under necessity
C) Because the meat became halal
D) Because the person did not intend to break the oath

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, necessity (idtarar) completely lifts the prohibition of haram acts, so no oath-breaking occurs; concessions (rukhsah) do not abrogate the original ruling (azimah).
